DTU. During my work on this thesis, I encountered some difficulties and obstacles due to personal circum-
stances. Overcoming these obstacles were, so far, one of my life’s greatest struggles. Completing my work on

fessional skills, but more importantly been symbolic for my path through life. Improving myself, learning from
my mistakes and taking control of my circumstances.

==PAGE:4==
Abstract
The Danish government has a future target of reducing CO2 emissions in the Danish energy production and
distribution network. To achieve this goal, large heat pumps are expected to be a central part of the future dis-
trict heating network, as it has the capacity to regulate the wind power in the electricity network by utilizing
district heating, and support sustainable biomass in the green conversion of the heat production and distribu-
tion. There are no official nationwide plans regarding large scale heat pumps, but there is an understanding that
the combination of biomass, solar and wind energy alone will not be sufficient in the long run.
HP’s are especially relevant in the context of the Greater Copenhagen Area (GCA), where it is a stated goal
to be CO2 neutral in 2025. This goal is mainly reached by conversion to biomass. But as sustainable biomass
is not an unlimited resource and prices may rise, other sources of renewable heat are considered. In “Heat Plan
Greater Copenhagen 3” large HP’s are expected to contribute up to 250 – 300 MW heat capacity before 2035
and around 600 MW towards 2050.
To accelerate knowledge and gain new insight in the operation of large heat pumps, two 5 MW heat pumps
will be developed and implemented in phase 2 of the SVAF project. One of these heat pumps will use ocean
water as a heat source. This project has aimed to model the heat pump in detail, in order to generate knowledge
of thermodynamic effects on a short time scale, during start up.
Modelling a heat pump to catch thermodynamic effects on a short time scale, requires a model of some com-
plexity, which is valid for a wide range of operation, from near thermodynamic equilibrium with surroundings
to full load capacity. The heat transfer occurring in the heat exchangers have been modelled in detail, where
geometry and flow characteristics have been accounted for. The compressors are modelled with varying effi-
ciencies based on pressure differential, while the state of vapour and liquid levels in the vessel, take vapour and
liquid flows into account, as well as liquid boiling.
Simple controls are implemented to regulate various process variables in the model
The full model formulation resulted in a model, where the heat transfer, state changes and flows could be
studied in detail, during scenarios of significant load changes. The results show, that start-up times could be
reduced significantly from 10 minutes to just over 5 minutes by increasing the capacity adjustment of the com-
pressors and water feed pump.
As large industrial heat pumps are integrated into the power grid and district heating network on a larger
scale, it becomes necessary to have knowledge and develop control methods, as the grid variability increases. As
more renewable energy is integrated into the grid, intelligent operation of the systems connected to the grid
and heat supply network becomes of greater importance. Having a model, on which control methods for load
changes and start-ups can be developed further will be of high significance.

1
Introduction - Heat Pumps and District
Heating
1.1 Project background
The Danish government has a future target of reducing CO2 emissions in the Danish energy production and
distribution network. To achieve this goal, large heat pumps (HP) are expected to be a central part of the future
district heating (DH) network, as it has the capacity to regulate the wind power in the electricity network by
utilizing DH, and support sustainable biomass in the green conversion of the heat production and distribu-
tion. There are no official nationwide plans regarding large scale HP’s, but there is an understanding that the
combination of biomass, solar and wind energy alone will not be sufficient in the long run.
HP’s are especially relevant in the context of the Greater Copenhagen Area (GCA), where it is a stated goal
to be CO2 neutral in 2025. This goal is mainly reached by conversion to biomass. But as sustainable biomass
is not an unlimited resource and prices may rise, other sources of renewable heat are considered. In “Heat Plan
Greater Copenhagen 3” large HP’s are expected to contribute up to 250 – 300 MW heat capacity before 2035
and around 600 MW towards 2050.
Large HP’s are facing a series of challenges that hinders a wider use. A major challenge is the feasibility of the
cost of investment. Particularly in areas with low cost of alternative heating, like in the GCA. Lack of knowledge
and experience in operating large scale HP’s in a wider system integration and operational expenses is also a
challenge to be addressed, especially in the case of using natural refrigerants (required by Danish law) supplying
heat above 70 °C.
To accelerate knowledge and use of HP’s for system integration and improved cost efficiency, two 5 MW test
plants are going to be developed and implemented in phase 2 of a research project titled “Experimental devel-
opment of electric heat pumps in the Greater Copenhagen DH system” (Danish title: “Store VArmepumper
til Fjernvarme” (SVAF)). The concepts will be based on geothermal and sewage/sea water, with a potential for
upscaling the technical concepts to 50-100 MW.
A vital part of HP’s being integrated with a larger system with renewable energy sources is SMART system
integration. The HP’s are run with electricity as the power source, with a fluctuating demand and production
on the electricity grid, as well as a fluctuating heat demand. It is thus important to develop methods for
Project statement
This thesis will address simulated smart grid operation of a heat pump, where a heat pump is started from an
’off’ state to ramp up to full heat load capacity. In future SMART Grid implementation of large scale heat
pumps in a variable demand and supply of power and heat, insight into operation of the heat pump on short
time scales is needed. A detailed model of a heat pump developed for testing will be formulated, which will be
able to form the groundwork for developing new control methods for large scale heat pump operation.
1
==PAGE:17==
• What are the start-up dynamics of a large scale heat pump on a short time scale?
• How quickly can a large scale heat pump be made to reach full load capacity and supply heat for a district
heat network
• How quickly does the heat pump change load, from full load operation
• How will the heat pump be affected by this operation
Task and methods
A detailed model of a heat pump is needed, to describe the effects occurring in the heat pump, as well as handle
operation of the heat pump over a wide load range. Detailed models for heat transfer in heat exchangers is
needed, as well as other components of the heat pumps.
The heat pump in question is part of the SVAF research project, which in its second phase will develop and
test operation of a large scale heat pump. The heat pump is sourced by ocean and waste water, where only the
ocean water will be used in this project. Total capacity of the heat pump is 5 MW, which is delivered to the
district heating network by two parallel two stage ammonia heat pumps.
An extensive literature study will be done, to secure newest research on evaporation and condensation for
ammonia in plate heat exchangers. Known correlations for the more extensively tested shell & tube heat ex-
changers will be used, while compressors will be formulated with variable efficiencies depending on operation
point. Other components in the heat pumps will be modelled in detail as required.
1.2 The basic Heat Pump
The vapour compression cycle of a heat pump is a reversed ’heat engine’ based on the theoretical concept of the
Carnot cycle, a thermodynamic cycle proposed by Sadi Carnot in 1824. In principle it works exactly the same
as a refrigeration cycle, moving heat from the inside of a cold storage to the ’hot’ outside ambient temperature.
A heat pump uses a heat source at low temperature of ample supply to supply heat for various purposes, from
domestic air-conditioning systems to industrial processes and district heating.
The Carnot cycle
The basic concept of the heat pump as a function is to convert low temperature heat energy into high temper-
ature heat energy. The underlying principle and theoretical function is based on the idealized reversible Carnot
cycle functioning in reverse. The Carnot cycle describes a process derived from the 1st and 2nd law of thermody-
namics. The 1st law stated simply that energy is never lost or destroyed, only transformed. The 2nd law stating
that heat cannot transfer from a cold to a hot body without adding work.
2
==PAGE:18==
Figure 1.1: The basic Carnot cycle in a T-s diagram
The Carnot cycle describes a ’heat engine’ where heat is transferred from a hot to a cold body, and in the pro-
cess generates work. The reverse Carnot cycle describes the process where work is added and heat is transferred
from the cold to the hot body. The basic heat pump and refrigeration cycle in principle.
• Isothermal expansion at low temperature, absorbing heat from surrounding heat source
• Isentropic compression from low to high temperature without heat transfer/losses (adiabatic)
• Isothermal compression at high temperature, rejecting heat to the surrounding heat sink
• Isentropic expansion from high to low temperature without heat transfer/losses (adiabatic)
The Carnot cycle describes an ideal process with no losses and reversibility. Actual cycles are irreversible,
which means heat and mechanical losses during the thermodynamic processes of the cycle.
Basic Heat Pump and components
Most heat pumps work by a mechanical vapour compression cycle, which in its most basic configuration, di-
rectly translate the reverse Carnot cycle into a working cycle. Other principles for moving heat from a cold to a
hot body by adding work exist. Such as the absorption cycle utilizing heat and liquid pumps for compression,
or the gas cycle operating without phase changes in the heat exchangers, obtaining a variable temperature glide.
Otherconceptsandcombinationsoftheseexist, butthebasicprinciplestillappliesaspertheCarnotcycle. Work
has to be added, to move heat from cold to hot.
The mechanical vapour compression cycle functions by utilizing the phase change inherent in all matter. In
these cases using refrigerants with suitable properties for the application. Following the principles of the reverse
Carnot cycle, the basic heat pump uses four main components. The evaporator, the compressor, the condenser
and the expansion valve.
3
==PAGE:19==
Figure 1.2: The basic heat pump cycle in a logP-h diagram
Emulating the process of the Carnot cycle
• Isothermal and isobaric phase change by absorbing heat from the heat source and evaporating the refrig-
erant into a state of vapour
• Volumetric compression work on the vapour, increasing pressure and temperature to a superheated state
• Isothermal and isobaric phase change by rejecting heat to the heat sink and condensing the refrigerant
into a state of liquid
• Volumetric expansion of the liquid, lowering the pressure and therefore the temperature as well in the
phase change area
It can be argued that the fifth and most important component is the refrigerant, which certainly is essential
for the functionality of the cycle as the medium carrying and transporting the energy in the cycle.
4
==PAGE:20==
Evaporator
 water
 ref
Tw,in
Tw,out
Xref,in
Tevap
Xref,out
  heat
Figure 1.3: Principal diagram of an evaporator
The refrigerant inside the evaporator absorbs the latent heat needed for vaporisation from the surrounding heat
source. As the refrigerant is in a state of initiated phase change, the temperature and pressure remains constant
while the refrigerant absorbs heat needed for vaporisation. The refrigerant flows into the evaporator from the
expansion valve, where it is throttled to reduce pressure to a temperature level lower than the heat source, for it
to be able to absorb heat. As the compressor runs it will maintain a pressure differential and a flow by sucking
vaporised refrigerant at the evaporator outlet. As the refrigerant continually expands due to evaporation the
evaporator is fed with more refrigerant from the expansion valve.
Compressor
 ref
href,in
href,out
Figure 1.4: Principål diagram of a compressor
Different concepts for the mechanical compression exist, and will be covered elsewhere. The compressor sucks
the vapour away from the evaporator, maintaining a low pressure for evaporation, compresses the vapour vol-
umetrically, pressurising it and generating heat in the process. Producing a superheated vapour with high pres-
sure, forcing the vapour into the condenser.
5
==PAGE:21==
In the Carnot cycle, the compression process is isentropic and adiabatic. In reality things are not that ideal,
the process is irreversible and not isentropic. In the theoretical cycle entropy is constant, but in reality entropy
at the outlet is higher than the theoretical ideal cycle. As the compression happens fast and by mechanical work,
there will be mechanical friction losses and heat losses to the surroundings during the process. It is helpful to
define an isentropic efficiency for the compressor, to describe the work input required for vapour compression,
based on the ratio between ideal work and actual work. This isentropic efficiency can be assumed constant
during steady state operation, but varies with varying load and pressure ratios for the compressor. Depending
on the insulation of the compressor, the process can sometimes be assumed adiabatic, but in other cases it is also
helpful to define a heat loss factor for the compressor.
As the compressor is working to compress and move refrigerant mass, it is also helpful to define a volumetric
efficiency for the compressor. As the refrigerant vapour is compressed and moved around, some refrigerant
will inevitable leak back into the compressor, instead of getting forced through the outlet of the compressor.
The volumetric efficiency is defined by the geometric swept compression volume which the compressor could
theoretically move per revolution, in relation to the actual refrigerant volume forced through the outlet per
compressor revolution.
Condenser
 water
 ref
Tw,in
Tw,out
Xref,in
Tcond
Xref,out
  heat
Figure 1.5: Principle diagram of a condenser
The condenser serves the purpose of rejecting the latent heat from the refrigerant during the condensation
phase, giving of heat from the condensation to the surrounding heat sink. As such the refrigerant is required
to have a higher temperature than the heat sink during the condensation, which in turn is determined by the
pressure. Most of the heat transfer occurs at a constant temperature due to the isothermal and isobaric nature of
thecondensationphasechange, butastherefrigerantexitsthecompressorinastateofsuperheatedvapour,some
of the heat transfer occurs while desuperheating the vapour to the condensation temperature. The condenser
might also cool the refrigerant liquid further after condensation, as this can have beneficial effects for the system
as a whole.
The pressure in the condenser is determined by the compressor forcing refrigerant into the condenser, and
the continual condensation of refrigerant in the condenser, as well as the restriction of the expansion valve at the
outlet of the condenser. As the restriction of the expansion valve varies, as well as the flow from the compressor
varies with the load, refrigerant mass and pressure in the condenser is affected accordingly.
6
==PAGE:22==
Expansion
 ref
href,in
href,out
Figure 1.6: Principle diagram of an expansion valve
Several different types of concepts for the expansion device between the condenser and evaporator exists, de-
pending on the application, but the purpose is always the same
• Throttling the refrigerant flow between the condenser and evaporator, maintain a pressure differential,
a high and low pressure side.
• Controlling the refrigerant flow to the evaporator, making sure that the evaporator is filled with liquid
for evaporation, but also ensuring that all of the refrigerant is evaporated before entering the compressor.
As the expansion device limits the liquid flow of refrigerant, the flow of refrigerant expands end reduces in
pressure. Reducing the pressure causes some of the refrigerant to vaporise. This portion must be supplied with
the latent heat of vaporisation, which can only come from the refrigerant itself, thus reducing the temperature.
The low temperature for evaporation in the system is created by the throttling process in the expansion device,
creatingtheboundarybetweenhighpressureforcondensation, andlowpressureforevaporationinconjunction
with the compressor.
No heat transfer occurs and no work is done in the expansion device, so the process is calculated with no
change of specific energy in the refrigerant.
Basic principles and deﬁnitions
When regarding the vapour compression cycle as an energy system, some general thermodynamic definitions
for efficiency are helpful for comparing systems of various configurations.
Carnot efficiency
The carnot efficiency for the vapour compression cycle, defines the maximum theoretical efficiency for the
load, that could be achieved with either a refrigeration or heat pump cycle. As the vapour compression cycle is a
machine that moves and converts heat by mechanical input, rather than creating work from heat, the efficiency
they are are able to attain is much higher. The distinction between ’high’ and ’low’ quality energy should be
noted here, as heat is considered low quality energy, while electric energy, used to generate work is considered
high quality energy.
7
==PAGE:23==
The carnot coefficient of performance (COP), is derived from Carnot’s theorem. For the refrigeration cy-
cle, the COPcool,canot is defined by the ratio of cold temperature TC achieved to the temperature difference
between the hot and cold reservoir TH −TC
COPcool,canot =
TC
TH −TC
(1.1)
and for the heat pump, the COPheat,carnot is defined by the ratio of hot temperature achieved to the tem-
perature difference
COPheat,carnot =
TH
TH −TC
(1.2)
It should seem obvious that the theoretical COPs should increase, as the temperature difference decreases.
Coefficient Of Performance
The real COP is defined by the ratio of the load to the mechanical work put in to the system. The real COP
will always be lower than the theoretical COP carnot.
For the refrigeration cycle it is defined as
COPcool =
ÛQcool
ÛWtotal
(1.3)
And for the heat pump cycle, the COP is defined by
COPheat =
ÛQheat
ÛWtotal
(1.4)
It should be noted, that the COP for a heat pump will always be higher than for a refrigeration cycle. For
the refrigeration cycle, the added heat by compression is basically additional unwanted heat, which the system
needs to reject, while for the heat pump the added heat from compression is beneficial for the efficiency of the
system.
For a basic vapour compression cycle, the function of refrigeration and heating is reversible. The cycle serves
thesamefundamentalpurposeofabsorbingheat, thuscoolingthesurroundingsoftheevaporator, andrejecting
that heat to a heat sink surrounding the condenser. For the same vapour compress, and from the relation that
ÛQheat −ÛQcool = ÛWtotal, we get that COPheat when functioning as a heat pump is 1 higher than COPcool
when functioning as a refrigeration cycle.
COPheat −COPcool = 1
(1.5)
When the real COP and the theoretical COP carnot are known, an efficiency can be defined. The efficiency
of vapour compression cycle measured against the theoretical coefficient of performance can be useful for com-
parison, but is seldom used as COP is most commonly used in the industry.
ηth =
COP
COPcarnot
(1.6)
The basic heat pump cycle can be seen in Figure 1.7, with the ratios of ÛQheat = ÛQcool + ÛWtotal
8
==PAGE:24==
  cool
  heat
 comp
Subcooling
Superheating
Figure 1.7: The basic heat pump cycle
Superheating and subcooling
Superheating and subcooling are useful for various purposes. Subcooling the refrigerant further after con-
densation, is useful for increasing the performance of the cycle. For the heat pump, more heat potential is
delivered to the heat sink, while for the refrigeration cycle the cooling potential for the cycle is increased, as
the distance, the difference of the speciﬁc enthalpies of the cycle are increased, with massflow multiplied by the
enthalpy differences giving the heating and cooling rate.
Superheating refrigerant vapour can be done as well, intentionally or unintentionally, before entering the
compressor. The benefit of superheating is primarily to ensure that the refrigerant entering the compressor is
completely evaporated, as non-evaporated droplets of liquid can increase wear on the compressor. The benefits
from subcooling can be somewhat the same, as the potential for heating or cooling is increased, but this is easier
achieved with subcooling. Superheating can be beneficial for the compressor, depending on the refrigerant
properties, as the compressor might be able to transfer mechanical work to the vapour more effectively. This
depends largely on the refrigerant and type of compressor, though.
1.3 Copenhagen District Heating Network
There’s a long history of district heating in Denmark, starting from the 1898’s with a mounting problem with
waste in the municipality of Frederiksberg. The growing town at the time, was seeking solutions for getting
rid of waste, as land areas were getting more expensive [1]. With inspiration from Germany, the first waste
incineration plant was established in 1903, providing both heat and electricity. With the success of the waste
incineration plant in Frederiksberg, other communities were subsequently inspired to build plants as well.
The development continued up through the years, mainly in conjunction with the establishment of large
housing areas. Due to the second World War there was a lack of diesel oil, and some plant owners were forced
to build solid-fuel heat-only boilers to provide heating. As the oil supply was normalised again, the increased
excess capacity from the boilers provided an opportunity for extending the heating supply networks.
From the 1960’s forward, Denmark already had more than 50 years of experience in district heating, and
9
==PAGE:25==
based on an idea by some suppliers to develop towns and district heating with a low supply temperature of
90°℃. The major breakthrough came, when revolutionary piping was developed with iron piping, covered with
polyurethane insulation and a dense plastic casing. This reduced problems with corrosion while minimizing
heat loss. Furthermore electrodes were built in to the insulation layer to detect problems with leakage and mois-
ture, making the problem of replacing the piping easy. With an improvement in material the possible supply
temperature was raised to 120°℃, which further made it possible to use waste heat from industrial processes.
The development of district heating in Denmark up until this point, was quite similar to other countries.
But as the energy crisis hit in 1973, Denmark was facing a huge economical crisis, as the country was almost
100% dependable on imported fossil fuels for heating. A plan was devised to save fuels and reduce consumer
costs.rigsfællesskabet til debat
• Systematic planning of the heat supply in all areas
• Greatest possible CHP generation on the basis of such planning
• Insulation of buildings
• Development of highly effective district heating pipe systems, pre-insulated pipes with high insulation
properties and low installation costs
• Reduction of operating temperatures in district heating systems and a variable flow in the pipelines to
secure the most economical operation in the overall production and distribution system, while at the
same time allowing the possibility to apply pre-insulated pipes
This plan has greatly increased the effectiveness of heating in major parts of Denmark, and reduced the overall
energy consumption per inhabitant for space heating. Today, about 63% of Danish households are supplied via
district heating systems using, for a large part, environmentally clean and energy efficient heat supplies.
District Heating of Greater Copenhagen
The District Heating Network of Greater Copenhagen cover a wide area of central Copenhagen and surround-
ing urban areas. It consists of a large network of piping, carrying hot water or steam for distribution of heat for
domestic, office and industrial use. The system is outlined in figure 1.8
10
==PAGE:26==
Figure 1.8: Greater Copenhagen District Heating Network. Courtesy of Rambøll
The district heating network of greater Copenhagen is a world known case study in effective use and redistri-
bution of heat from centralized processes and waste heat. The system uses hot water as a heat carrying medium
and consists of 180 km of primary 250-800mm diameter distribution piping and 1500 km of secondary small
bore distribution piping, of which 130 km is used for steam distribution [2, 3, 4]. By using centralized (and
larger decentralized) processes, combined with minimal heat losses during distribution in the piping, the over-
all fuel efficiency is increased and fuel consumption is reduced by about 30% [3], as the centralized process is
optimized for a very high efficiency compared to many small oil burners or other methods for domestic heating.
Heating related CO2 emissions are estimated to be reduced by 40-50%.
The district heating network is owned and operated by four companies working together to supply water,
heating, cooling, watertreatmentandutilityforthegreaterCopenhagenarea. Thesystemasawholeiscomputer
controlled from an operations centre in Frederiksberg, run by CTR.
• CTR - Responsible for transmitting heat to the areas in white, and consisting of the municipalities of
Copenhagen, Frederiksberg, Tårnby, Gladsaxe and Gentofte. Furthermore they’re responsible for the
planning, establishment, funding and further development of the whole primary distribution system.
• VEKS - Responsible for supplying and transmitting heat to the areas in grey, consisting of the munici-
palities of Albertslund, Brøndby, Glostrup, Greve, Hvidovre, Høje-Taastrup, Ishøj, Køge, Roskilde, Rø-
dovre, Solrød and Vallensbæk.
• HOFOR - Responsible for utility, water, water treatment, heat, cooling, steam amongst other things.
They’re a collection of companies with different functions, owned by Copenhagen. They were formerly
named Copenhagen Energy and merged with the water distribution and treatment companies of Albert-
slund, Brøndby, Dragør, Herlev, Hvidovre, Rødovre and Vallensbæk. They own and operate the steam
11
==PAGE:27==
distribution system shown in pale blue (due for conversion to hot water) as well as the small bore distri-
bution system for the whole system. They’re a partner in CTR.
• Vestforbrænding - Responsible for the waste management, including collection, recycling and waste-to-
energy,generatingheatandsupplyingthemunicipalitiesofHerlev,Ballerup,Gladsaxe,FuresøandLyngby-
Taarbæk. Shown on the map in dark blue are the municipalities of Herlev and Ballerup. Moreover Vest-
forbræning interconnects Hilleerød and Farum-værløse in Furesø, and the system will be interconnected
with DTU and the district heating company Holte Fjernvarme in the future [2].
The whole system is supplied with heat from various sources, ranging from Combined Heat & Power plants
(CHP), waste heat, waste incineration, geothermal heat, heat pumps and boilers. The system provides provides
98% of Copenhagen city itself with heat, while Copenhagen accounts for 70% of the total heat from the entire
system network [4]. The total heated floor area provided from the interconnected system i 75 mio. m2 and the
annual production of heat is 30,000 TJ. The average emission is 100kgCO2/MWh. The heat production for
the network comes from [2]:
• 2 large biomass fuelled CHP plants: Amagerværket and Avedøreværket
• 1 biomass fuelled industrial CHP plant in Køge, owned by VEKS
• 3 waste-to-energy CHP plants: ARC, Vestforbrænding and KARA/NOVEREN
• Geothermal heat and surplus heat
• Sludge incinerators at two waste water treatment plants owned by Biofos
• 30 MW gas fuelled combined Cycle CHP plant at DTU owned by DC, mainly operating as peak capacity
in the power system
• 5 MW waste heat from the pectine factory CPKelco
• Several heat pumps for cogeneration of heating and cooling
• Electric boilers
• 50 gas/oil fuelled peak load and spare capacity boilers
Furthermore, there is in total 3 x 24,000 m3 heat storage tanks for optimization and flexibility of the system.
More heat storage capacity is in the pipeline for the future, including ATES systems and heat storage pits for
increased flexibility, optimization and supply security of the system. By using heat exchangers, variable flow and
speed controlled liquid pumps it is ensured that the hot water goes where it is needed and in the exact amounts
it is needed, while maintaining high efficiency for the power needed to maintain the flow.
The reason why the district heating project has become such a huge success can be attributed to various rea-
sons. Tax incentives, planning regulations, mandatory connection of households if district heating is available,
price reductions and advanced control systems [4]. In 1979 a new heat supply act was implemented, starting
the planning process for the development, zoning various supply areas, making it mandatory for households
to connect to the network, while at the same time reducing the price. Tax incentives were introduced for the
electricity companies in the mid 80’s, meaning they paid less fuel tax if CHP (Combined Heat & Power) plants
were used, enabling the reduction of heat cost for the consumer. The pricing for the consumer is greatly re-
duced today, compared with the cost of heating with a separate oil burner. Annual cost for heating in the case
of a 130 m2 home is 11,342 DKK (1500 EUR) for district heating, compared with 22,000 DKK (2900 EUR) for
individual heating, saving 10,658 DKK (1400 EUR) in heating cost annually.
The results of all this development in district heating can be summarized:
12
==PAGE:28==
• Today the district heating network covers 98% of the total heating needs of the Copenhagen City - the
equivalent of a floor area around 50 million square meters
• The CTR and VEKS network also provides 15% of the total Danish heat requirements.
• About 30% of the annual district heating demand is covered with surplus heat from waste incineration,
and the remaining production of district heating is based on geothermal energy and fuels as wood pellets,
straw, straw pellets, natural gas, oil and coal.
• About 80% of the CO2 emissions in Copenhagen result from the consumption of heat and electricity
• CO2 emissions have dropped by 187,600 tons annually from 3,460,000 tons in 1995 to 2,522,000 in
2000. Sulphur dioxide emissions have also been reduced by one third.
• In 2005 the entire district heating system replaced the equivalent of 290,000 tons of oil annually – that’s
950,040 tons CO2e emissions avoided. Copenhagen represents 70% of the system, which equals 203,000
tons of oil or 665,000 tons CO2e emissions avoided.
Allinall, thegreaterCopenhagenDistrictHeatingNetworkisanexcellentcasestudyinthebenefitsandeffec-
tiveness of a flexible and distributed collective heating system for domestic, office and industrial heating needs.
The interconnected network provides the possibility for updating the heat production sources and supply, as
technologies develop and new regulation stimulate the economic feasibility of using more renewable and envi-
ronmentally friendly heat production methods. It is also a great example of what can be achieved with strong
central political backing and local support with right incentives for a private market for transitioning to more
efficient, environmentally and consumer friendly technology.
In the future, the city of Copenhagen aims to become the worlds first CO2 neutral city, by fully converting
to sustainable biomass use and introducing heat pumps and more heat storage capacity into the system.
As a final comment, it is worth noting the recent development in district cooling by HOFOR [5]. The
cooling is provided by seawater intake, as well as absorption and compression chillers when there’s a surplus of
energy in the district heating during summer. In principle, the district cooling system works exactly the same as
district heating. The cold water for heat absorption is cooled at central installations, and distributed by piping
wheretheheatabsorptionisrequired, replacinglocalcoolingfacilitiesandairconditionersthusfreeingupspace,
while increasing supply security and reducing consumer costs. HOFOR is aiming at creating a district cooling
network far more efficient than conventional air conditioning, targeting office spacing and server room, etc. By
doing this they’re aiming to reduce cooling costs by 40% and CO2 emissions by 70% for companies switching
to district cooling. When the planned district cooling network has been finished for the entire capital, the aim
is reducing total emission of CO2 by 30,000 tonnes annually. It is easy to imagine heat pumps in a combined
district heating and cooling network covering both heating and cooling needs simultaneously
1.4 SVAF Project
The SVAF project is a direct consequence of the ambitions to make the Greater Copenhagen District Heating
Network’s supply of heating CO2 neutral by 2025, and the overall goal for the Danish government to be in-
dependent of fossil fuels, coal, oil and gas in 2050 [6]. The political goals for Denmark as a whole has been
outlined on the basis of the recent decades of research and political awareness of the impact from burning fossil
fuels for energy consumption and the development from heavy industrialization. Countries globally are focus-
ing on developing new methods to reduce consumption of fossil fuels and reduce emission of greenhouse gasses
and other pollutants. The Danish policy act are outlined in the following section.
13
==PAGE:29==

To achieve these targets, it is necessary with a comprehensive plan outlining the research, development and

==PAGE:30==
and development of larger and more powerful windmills and integrating them into the power grid for several
decades. Denmark has a strong profile as a world leader in sustainable energy [6], and policies for reducing the
dependency of fossil fuels are part of a natural political decision process.
The implementation of new technologies and renewable energy sources is supported economically by sub-
sidies, taxes and support schemes. The need for the development of a flexible, strong power grid, with high
capacity, energy storage and variable energy production has been outlined in the roadmap towards 2050, along
with the implementation of heat pumps, biomass, wind and solar. Energy companies and universities are cre-
ating strong collaborative networks for fuelling the research in renewable technology. The roadmap towards
2050 is outlined in Appendix D.
Heat plan Greater Copenhagen
As part of their plans be independent of fossil fuels in 2025 [8], the Copenhagen municipalities have mapped
their energy network to determine what direction the development should take in the future. Part of these
process has been Heat Plan Greater Copenhagen, which has run in three project phases so far. First phase of the
project, HeatPlanGreaterCopenhagen1[9]wasinitiatedbytheheatingcompaniesVEKS,CopenhagenEnergy
(nowHOFOR)andCTRtodetermineanoverallplanforthedevelopmentofdistrictheatingsupply. Thefocus
of the project was to determine which heat sources would be economically and environmentally attractive to
be integrated with the district heatings system, including the integration of small-scale technologies with CHP.
Different scenarios were analysed towards 2025 with perspectives towards 2050. The project was carried out
in April 2008 to June 2009. Main conclusions from phase 1 were, that renewable energy can cover 70 District
Heating Supplies by 2015 without negative economic consequences. To achieve this, two main initiatives were
necessary. Converting from fossil to biomass in central heating plants and utilizing increased waste volumes in
highly effective incinerators for electric and heat [10].
Second phase, Heat Plan Greater Copenhagen 2 [11], aimed to determine a more concrete plan for the fu-
ture development. The project was again carried out by VEKS, Copenhagen Energy(now HOFOR) and CTR,
assisted by Ea Energy analyses, and three scenarios were proposed for the development of heat supply in Copen-
hagen. The analyses were carried out in 2011 and completed in September. Main findings were how to achieve
CO2 neutrality by 2025.
• Reference scenario - continuing the current trend, Two of the major coal-fired power plants are converted
to biomass firing.
• Low-bio scenario - Conversion of the above plants is not performed
• CO2 neutral scenario - Providing a way to make Greater Copenhagen CO2 neutral by 2025
The third and current phase of Heat Plan Greater Copenhagen was carried out from October 2012 to Oc-
tober 2014 [12]. The companies involved were CTR, HOFOR and VEKS. The third project builds heavily on
the findings of the previous projects, and gives some further perspective on the development of the heat market
towards 2050. The short term focus of the development of heat supply is to focus on conversion to biomass at
CHP’s if the current pricing is to be maintained while the climate goals are reached. It is also stressed that alter-
native technologies must be integrated in the system. Some alternatives would be geothermal energy and heat
pumps integrated with an electrical grid relying more on wind energy, securing supply and reducing biomass
consumption [10].
Some key points from the findings of the third phase of the heat plan
• Drawing a perspective scenario for district heating towards 2050, including an assessment of the long-
term role of biomass in district heating.
• Analysing the development of the heating market up to 2050
15
==PAGE:31==
• Providing basics for evaluating possible increased connectivity in the heating networks among others in
Northern Zealand
• Analysing and quantifying the possibilities to contribute to integration of fluctuating electricity produc-
tion in the future energy system
With the development of these long term heat plans, it has been possible for the heating companies and
municipalities to clearly communicate the roadmap and long term investments for the future.
The SVAF project
The SVAF project has come about as a direct consequence of the plans to integrate alternative heat sources into
the future heat supply of the Greater Copenhagen District Heating Network. Participants in the project are
HOFOR, VEKS, CTR, Innoterm, Alfa Laval, Biofos, DME, DTU and Teknologisk Institut
The project name, SVAF, is an abbreviation of the project name in Danish, Store VArmepumper til Fjern-
varme (tr. Large Heat Pumps for District Heating). The aim of the SVAF project is to accelerate the use of,
and gather knowledge and experience with the operation and experimental development of large electric heat
pumps. Large Heat pumps in Denmark are, by regulation, only allowed to use natural refrigerant, due to the
global warming and ozone depletion potential of synthetic refrigerant. Synthetic refrigerants are only allowed
for smaller charge systems. The heat pumps are at first going to be two heat pumps, each with a capacity for
heat rejection of 5 MW, both heat pumps with a low temperature heat source, one with sea/sewage water and
the other by geothermal heat, able to lift and deliver the heat at a reasonably high temperature for district water
heating. The aim of the project is to collect experience and knowledge for up-scaling the concept of the heat
pumps to delivering a heat capacity of 50-100 MW each. The project has run in two phases so far.
The first phase of the project explored the heat source potential, possible optimal heat pump design configu-
rations and scenarios [13]. During the project phase, some of the cases approached were design criteria, how to
approach SMART grid integration, cost efficiency and maximal climate benefit. The findings from the project
concerned design criteria, choice of technology along with preliminary principle diagram and technical draw-
ings and draft of connections to heat and power networks. The first phase of the project ran from 2014 to 2015
and had project budget of 3.96 mio. DKK [13].
The second phase of the project is currently running [14]. The project seeks to clarify the barriers for in-
troduction of heat pumps into the overall heat supply. Especially the economic feasibility for heat pumps and
commercialisation of the technology. It aims to do this by installing and operating the two heats pumps at two
locations in the Greater Copenhagen area. The geothermal heat pump is to be located at Amager power station,
wherea geothermalwellis locatedandwillbe usedasaheatsource. Theotherheatpumpwilltestseaandsewage
water for use as a heat source and will be located at Sjællandsbroen. Optimization problems will be clarified and
knowledge about the operation of the heat pumps in a variable electric market will be obtained. The second
phase of the project will be running from 2016 to 2021 and has budget of 110 mio. DKK [14].
In low heat cost area, as the one in Copenhagen, the efficient district heating is ironically a challenge for the
economic feasibility of implementing the heat pumps, as the biomass CHP’s they’re intended to supplement
are supported by subsidies, while electric heat pumps are yet to be supported by subsidies. It is thought that, as
more operational knowledge of large heat pumps is obtained and proof of concept is established, the support
schemes will adjust accordingly.
1.5 Heat Sources
When considering a heat pumps, the purpose is to convert low temperature heat energy into usable high tem-
perature heat energy. This is accomplished by heat absorption from a heat source, forcing refrigerant to be
16
==PAGE:32==
evaporated. Compression of the evaporated refrigerant vapour to a high temperature, then rejecting the heat
from that high temperature refrigerant to a heat sink, forcing a condensation of the refrigerant. The net work
plus heat input into the closed system by heat absorption and compressor work is equal to the net heat rejected
from the condensation, equal to our heat pump capacity. A heat source is needed and the heat sources are
various and plentiful.
In most cases, if not all cases, a heat source with as high as possible a temperature is wanted. This is due to
the pressure difference of the high and low pressure level of the heat pump. As mentioned in the earlier sec-
tion 1.2, the evaporation and condensation of the refrigerant are isobaric processes, proceeding along constant
pressure. A higher temperature of the heat source, means the pressure difference between evaporation and con-
densation will be smaller, meaning more efficient, less work for the compressor to preform the temperature lift,
if dimensioned correctly for the system.
The temperature, however, is not the only important factor for the heat source. Capacity of the heat source is
of course important as well. If the heat source is a geothermal well, for example, the capacity of the heat source
is restricted by the flow of the geothermal well, and how much the sediment can be cooled down, without
disrupting and possibly stopping the geothermal processes by cooling them down too much. Another example
for consideration could possibly be a small scale heat pump for domestic single house heating purposes, where
the heat source are pipes laid down beneath the lawn. The soil is heated by the sun, and the capacity of the heat
source is thus restricted by the area of the lawn, but also by how much the lawn can possibly be cooled down,
without freezing the soil, creating a frozen layer.
With air-sourced heat pumps, the capacity of the heat source would be limited by the surface area of the
evaporator on the air side, the fan speed for blowing air through the evaporator and the need for defrosting the
evaporator due to condensing water molecules in the air during cooling.
For large scale heat pumps, different heat sources are considered possible.
• Ocean water - Extracting heat from ocean water from depths with a relatively stable temperature
• Air - Extracting heat from air by convection, blowing air through an evaporator with a large surface area
• Geothermal processes - Drilling holes in bedrock to access heat sources from geothermal processes
• Waste heat - Utilizing low temperature waste heat from various industrial processes or CHP’s
• Sewage water - Using the heat from sewage water, which is otherwise not utilized
• Heat storages - Possibly storing surplus heat at a low temperature for later conversion to high temperature
if feasible
• Cooling needs - If there is a cooling need available close-by, the refrigeration cycle could essentially be used
as a heat pump or vice versa, as the cycle is essentially identical
Otherheatsourcesexists, andanattemptofmakinganexhaustivelistofeverypotentiallyavailableheatsource
willnotbemadehere. Theessentialsoftheheatsourceisavailabilityandfeasibilityofinstallation. Parametersto
lookoutforare; easeofaccessforbothheatsourceanddeliveryofheatsupply, capacity, temperature, installation
needs for evaporator.
Each heat source presents its own challenges and technical solutions for extracting the needed heat for evap-
oration, which all plays into the feasibility of the heat pump itself, thus demanding careful consideration.
Seawater/Waste water
Seawater as a heat source for large scale heat pumps has been demonstrated at a number of installations in the
Nordic regions, such as in Drammen and Nordfjordeid in Norway and Vestmannaeyjar in Iceland
17
==PAGE:33==
Different configurations for using the ocean water as a heat source is possible, as seen in some of the examples.
Water can be pumped from a low water depth, where the temperature is reasonably stable during the seasons.
The water can be distributed by pipes for use in separate domestic heat pumps or used in centralized or de-
centralized large scale heat pumps for distribution by district heating, as in the examples in figure 1.9. Another
possible configuration, could be to put the heat exchanger directly in the ocean rather than pumping the water
up from a depth.
(a) Separate domestic heat pumps
(b) Decentralized large heat pump
(c) Centralized large heat pump
Figure 1.9: Heat from ocean water configurations
When using ocean water, the heat potential should be carefully considered as well as fouling potential for
the heat exchanger. As water temperature in most user cases is rather low, there is a lower limit to how much
heat can be extracted, before the water starts to freeze. This also depends on the salinity of the water, as higher
salinity lowers the freezing point. If the water has an initial low temperature, the flow of water can be increased,
to reduce the heat extracted per mass unit of water, thus reducing the cooling, but increasing the energy demand
for pumping work.
Waste water as a heat source, works in many ways by the same principles as water, and potentially has a en-
ergy content (temperature) than ocean water. Foreseeable issues with waste water is potentially availability and
higher risk of issues with fouling in the heat exchanger than with ocean water.
18
==PAGE:34==
2
Method - Heat Pump system modelling
2.1 Modelling the heat pump
Modelling a theoretical simulation model of a thermodynamic energy system can be approached in multiple
different ways, with varying degrees of complexity or simplification and assumptions. This thesis is attempting
to cover a large scale heat pump during start-up, which means it should be able to cover a very wide range of
thermodynamic state changes. Capturing the state changes throughout the system requires a higher degree of
detailed modelling throughout.
The typical approach will be to treat each component of the heat pump as control volumes, describing the
physical phenomena taking place in each component. For each control volume will be established mass flow
and energy balances, and each corresponding component will be connected by nodes. Inlet and outlet states of
the components will then be defined as variables by the foregoing and following component, creating a system
of equations.
The SVAF II GEA Heat Pump
The heat pump from GEA described in this thesis for the SVAF project, is comprised of two parallel two-stage
heat pumps, with a flooded evaporator and open inter-cooler vessel. The compressors are of the screw com-
pressor type. The evaporator and condenser are compact plate heat exchangers, while the desuperheaters and
subcoolers are of the shell & tube type. Furthermore oil separators are installed for the screw compressors, with
exchangers for cooling the oil and supplying additional heating to the district water supply.
While the inner cycle handles the boiling, compression, cooling and condensing of the refrigerant, the net-
work of heat exchangers comprised of the desuperheaters, condensers, subcoolers and oilcoolers for the com-
pressors handles the heating of water for the district heating network. A total of 12 heat exchangers handle the
heating of water. Each heat pump 6 heat exchangers in total. One condenser, two desuperheaters (one for each
compression stage), one subcooler and two oilcoolers (likewise one for each compression stage). The first heat
pump with a lower condensation pressure delivers a large part of the initial heating of the district water, while
the second heat pump at a higher condensation pressure primarily deliver the last part of heating to supply
temperature.

An overview of the heat pump system from the refrigerant side is provided in figure 2.1.

Figure 2.1: SVAF Heat pump Refrigerant circuit
Heat pump 1 is used as reference to describe the internal process of refrigerant in the heat pump.
The heat source is supplied by seawater in a PHE evaporator at 1 , which is supplied with liquid refrigerant
from the liquid separator at 2 and returns a mixture of partly evaporated refrigerant. The liquid separator con-
tains a mixture of vapour and liquid and is supplied with liquid from the intercooler at 6 , by a valve controlled
by the level of liquid. The vapour from the liquid separator is sucked in to the low stage screw compressor at
3 and compressed to a state of higher temperature and pressure. As the screw compressor is lubricated and
cooled with oil, the hot oil gets separated and cooled down at 4 . The superheated refrigerant vapour is cooled
down in the low stage S&T desuperheater at 5 , before entering the intercooler at 6 , which contains a mixture
of liquid and vapour. The vapour from the intercooler is sucked in to the high stage screw compressor at 7
and compressed to an even higher pressure level, while oil for lubrication and cooling is separated at 8 . The
refrigerant vapour at high pressure and temperature is cooled to condensation temperature in the S&T desu-
perheater at 9 . Condensation takes place at 10 in a PHE condenser and finally the refrigerant is subcooled at
11 in a S&T subcooler before returning to the intercooler. A valve after the subcooler adjusts the condensation
pressure based on the liquid content out of the condenser at 10 . The cycle for the second heat pump, which is
identical in basic configuration, but with a higher condensation pressure of T = 80°℃is seen in Figure 2.2

Figure 2.2: Heat Pump 2 - Two stage compression with open intercooling
Heat pump 2 runs in parallel with a higher condensation pressure, but the internal flow process of refrigerant
is identical.

Capability & Purpose
The purpose of the model is to be able to cover and simulate a wide range of operational conditions and ther-
modynamic state changes. This demands a certain degree of detailed and in some cases complex modelling.
- The heat pump model should be able to simulate operation of the heat pump during start up, from a state
of inactivity and thermodynamic equilibrium with the surroundings, to full load.
- The heat pump should be able to simulate part load operation
- The heat pump should be able to simulate defined load changes from full load to part load and back again.
Model detail
In some cases modelling a heat pump only requires for it to be able to simulate steady state operations, where
certain simplifications can be assumed. In this case, as the heat pump have to simulate a wide range of load
situations, with changing states and flows in each components, a higher degree of detail is required. Each heat
exchanger has to be modelled with specific discretised heat transfer coefficients along the flow direction of the
fluids in the heat exchangers. The pressures in the vessels, such as the liquid separator and the open intercooler
can not be assumed to be constant, and required a variable liquid level and vapour energy state. The compressor
efficiency has to be expressed as dependent on the pressure differential it is working against, and valves have to
be able to regulate flows as required for proper operation. Capacities are variable and governed by set process
variables.
Component Information
GEA have supplied a budget quotation for the proposed heat pump, which have been used to get information
for modelling the heat pump. The document supplies important information about each component. Type,
material, dimension, operating conditions and so forth, which have been implemented during modelling. Some
information about detailed dimensioning and operation is lacking in the document, and have thus been as-
sumed if necessary. No information about GEA’s calculations for dimensioning have been available, so each
component have been dimensioned for being able to deliver the needed load in the heat pump.
2.2 Components
Theoretical approaches to modelling the heat pump system components, energy balances, two-phase condi-
tions, vapour and liquid phases, pressures, mass flows etc.
Refrigerants
Refrigerant in a vapour compression cycle should be carefully chosen to fit the required application. In the basic
scheme of a vapour compression cycle consisting of compression, condensation, expansion and evaporation, the
refrigerant can be validly argued to be the critical fifth component that determines choice of other components.
Choice of refrigerant depends on a couple of different properties, such as efficiency of temperature range for
application during evaporation and condensation, pressure range, boiling point and critical temperature, global
warming potential (GWP), ozone depletion potential (ODP) toxicity, flammability, composition, heat capacity
vs. density and availability of the refrigerant. It should also be considered if the chemical composition of the
refrigerant limits the choice of materials for the components in the system. For example, ammonia is highly
22
==PAGE:38==
Table 2.1: Refrigerant safety groups
Safety Group
Higher
Flammability
A3
B3
Lower

No
Flammability
A1
B1
Lower Toxicity
Higher Toxicity
reactantwithcopperandotheralloysinthepresenceofevenalittlewatermoisture,andsteelorironcomponents
should then be used instead to prevent corroding the components.
Therewasatime, whensyntheticrefrigerantscontainingchlorides(CFC/HCFC)wereprevalentinuse. They
were banned due to the compositions potential for depleting the atmospheres ozone layer (ODP). A group of
other synthetic refrigerants not containing chlorides (HFC) were developed, but these are under restricted use
in Denmark today (>10 kg charge), due to the compositions containing carbon, and thus an increased potential
for global warming (GWP). Regulation in the EU is in place, that will restrict further use of some these refrig-
erants in the future. Today synthetic refrigerants are mostly used for small charge refrigeration and heat pump
applications, but new synthetic refrigerants with a low GWP have been or are in development, which contain
no chloride and no carbon (HFO) or a blend of (HFO/HFC).
Today natural refrigerants, consisting of hydrocarbons and inorganic, used before the synthetics were devel-
oped, have been reintroduced as the main refrigerant in use for industrial and large scale applications. When
considering the natural refrigerants it comes down to comparing performance such as temperature range, effi-
ciency, heat capacity and density, as well as safety issues such as toxicity and flammability of the fluid.
The safety classification of a refrigerant is signified by a letter and a number e.g. ’A3’. The letter indicates
toxicity, A for low and B for high toxicity. The number indicates flammability, where 1 is no flame propagation,
2 is low flammability and 3 is high flammability. This all determines how the refrigerants should be handled in
application depending on risk level. An example could be to ask if should be an intermediary loop to reduce
risk of ammonia leakage in food applications. Another example could be to reduce the risk of flammability if
there’s a propane leakage and a spark should occur.
When considering performance, one of the most important properties to consider is the temperature range.
The refrigerant should be able to cover the temperature range that the application is working at. It should be
able to provide the necessary temperature differentials. The heat capacity of the refrigerant determines how
much heat can be absorbed per mass unit of fluid. A low heat capacity will demand higher volume flows and
thus larger components, while a high heat capacity will demand smaller components.
The application described in this thesis uses ammonia as a refrigerant. Ammonia is characterized by its high
specific heat capacity compared with other refrigerants, thus requiring smaller components. The efficiency is
quite good and its very low in price with no ODP and GWP. It is a very suitable refrigerant for large plants due
to this.
There are some drawbacks with ammonia however. The refrigerant has a high isentropic exponent resulting
in high discharge temperatures from the compressor.
23
==PAGE:39==
Phase change
For the desuperheaters and subcoolers, heat transfer equations could be stated with temperatures only, with
the assumption that the states are either saturated vapour or liquid. During heat transfer in an evaporator of
condenser, no such assumption can be taken. For saturated vapour and liquid, the properties of the fluid stay
relativelyconstantandcanbeestimatedwithsomecertainty. Butastheflowpropertiesoftherefrigerantchanges
significantly during the phase change. As shown in Figure 2.4, the flow regimes change along the length of the
evaporator.
Figure 2.4: Different flow regimes during phase change
This has a significant influence on the method of mechanics of heat transfer, and is strongly dependent on
the vapour quality, proven in numerous experiments and as such most if not all predictor methods depend on
the vapour quality. The changing nature of heat transfer in the fluid is seen in Figure 2.5, changing along with
the vapour quality. Which boiling mechanic is dominant in the fluid during phase change, is also dependent on
the mass flux, as lover mass fluxes typically dependent more on nucleate boiling, while high mass fluxes depend
more heavily on convective boiling.
24
==PAGE:40==
Figure 2.5: Boiling regimes for water during phase change
The heat flux as dependent on the boiling regime is shown in Figure 2.6 as well.
Figure 2.6: Heat transfer coefficient vs. vapour quality
25
==PAGE:41==
Modelling refrigerants and use in simulation
When modelling a heat pump application, it is necessary to use a language and modelling environment that
facilitates the use of table values and interpolation for refrigerant properties at differing states. EES from F-
Chart and Refprop for the Matlab programming language are examples of this.
In EES, there is an in-built function for calling the state of refrigerant, given information for the state is oth-
erwise available, based on assumptions, energy balances or state change equations. Taking the liquid separator
as an example, Based on an assumption of pressure equilibrium in the vessel, the system of equations gives the
overall pressure inside the liquid separator from a function call for pressure, based on the specific volume and
energy content of the vapour. Assuming pressure equilibrium, the overall pressure of the liquid separator and
thus the evaporation pressure/temperature is known, as well as the inlet state for the compressor, where it is
useful to know the enthalpy of the vapour, when calculating the compressor process work.
Another example would be, during the evaporation or condensation process, where temperature and pres-
sure is constant. In this instance, no information about the energy state could be found, given only the pressure
and temperature. Either the enthalpy or vapour quality would have to be known, for the state of refrigerant to
be estimated.
Compressors
Compressors come in several different types as described in Granryd [15, Chapter 7]. The basic function and
concept of the compressor is to transfer mechanical energy to a gas contained in a volume, by compressing the
volume, which will put pressure on the gas and heat it up to a higher energy state. Different concepts exists for
this purpose giving name to the different types. Reciprocating, screw, rotary, scroll and turbo compressors. Each
type has its benefits and drawbacks and which one to choose depends on the application. Some factors are to be
considered. Manufacturing complexity and construction, wear and tear during use, mechanical and volumetric
efficiencies and losses.
Most of the compressors are of the displacement type, meaning that they compress a volume containing gas,
as mentioned earlier. The turbo compressor works by a different concept, mainly by transferring kinetic energy
(speed)totherefrigerantbyanimpeller. Itworksbycentrifugalforcesandincreasespressurebyadiffuseroutlet.
The compressor concepts can be further divided in different types depending on their drive motor config-
uration. Hermetic, semi-hermetic or open types. In an open design the drive motor is placed outside of the
refrigerant volume and the mechanical energy is transferred by a shaft, necessitating a shaft seal. These seals are
almost perfectly tight in operation, but run the risk of refrigerant leakage, especially after long periods with-
out use. Hermetic compressors, where the drive motor is contained inside the refrigerant volume have been
developed to prevent this. Hermetic types have been welded shut, while semi-hermetic are bolted and can be
opened without damaging the shell. The hermetic come in different configurations, as some refrigerant prop-
erties and/or oil can prove problematic for the drive motor, or prevent access for cooling of the drive.
When considering the displacement type compressors, it is useful to define the swept volume of the com-
pressor, describing the total volume of refrigerant displaced in one revolution of mechanical work. The actual
volume of refrigerant displaced will be smaller than the geometrical volume of the compressor, which makes
it useful to describe a volumetric eﬃciency, which is inherently linked with the pressure differential over the
compressor inlet and outlet. Figure 2.7 shows the volumetric efficiency for different compressor types
26
==PAGE:42==
Figure 2.7: Flow characteristics of different compressor types
The higher the pressure ratio, the lower the volumetric efficiency. Volumetric and isentropic (mechanical)
efficiency are highly dependent on the compressor design and size, but will not be described in detail here. Ta-
ble 2.2 shows the typical volumetric flows for different types of compressors
Table 2.2: Typical flow capacities for different compressor types

Only the screw compressor will be described in this approach to modelling, as this is the one being used for
the application in the project. The screw compressor comes in one of two configurations, one screw with gate
rotors or two interlocking (male/female) screw profiles. The Heat Pump in this application uses a two screw
configuration.
27
==PAGE:43==
Figure 2.8: Rotary twin screw compressor
With two interlocking screws, they are shaped to create several closed volumes between the screws where the
refrigerant vapour will flow in to, and along the screw chamber reducing in volume following the rotation, up
until a discharge port at the end of the chamber. The discharge ports geometric shape and size will determine
a "built-in pressure ratio" for the compressor, or more appropriately a "built-in volume ratio". This built-in
volume ratio of the compressor will determine both its mechanical and volumetric effectiveness, as the built-in
ratio of the compressor might differ from the pressure ratio which the system application operates at. Consider
the pV diagram shown in figure 2.9 where a compression of volume νi from pressure a to exit pressure at b is
shown. If the built-in volume ratio corresponds to the exit pressure at b, the process would proceed smoothly.
If the compression reaches too high a pressure at b1 a gas shock wave will be formed when the gas flows into
the discharge line. If the compression however is too low at pressure b2, gas from the discharge line would
flow rapidly back into the compression volume. the shaded areas in the diagram represents lost work from a
mismatch of built-in and system pressure ratios.
Figure 2.9: pV diagram for compression processes with different built-in volume ratios
28
==PAGE:44==

Some assumptions have to be taken for the compressor, for use in simulation. The nominal compressor RPM
is given from data, as well as the swept volume per revolution, which is known from the manufacturers, GEA
compressors. From Appendix C, the compressor model and type is detailed, along with the heat rating, based
on which an assumption of the specific compressor is made, giving the swept volume dimensions.

As the expression for the isentropic efficiency in equation 2.1 estimated the ideal isentropic efficiency, a max-
imum isentropic efficiency of ηcomp = 0.9 is assumed, which is factored to the expression.
Once the isentropic and volumetric efficiency of the compressor can be estimated based on pressure ratio of
the system and compressor, the actual volume flow and work for compression can be estimated from the in-
let state at suction and geometrical swept volume of the compressor, given the compressor revolutions. The
isentropic efficiency of the compressor is used to estimate how much mechanical work is transferred to the re-
frigerant vapour during compression, but oil cooling in the compressor should be accounted for as well.
Assumptions for the compressors
• Isentropic compression process
• Maximum ηcomp = 0.9
• Oil cooled compression process
• Volumetric inner compression ratio dimensioned to the projected pressure ratios at the various heat
pump stages
• Constant polytropic exponent
• Swept volume from data sheets of manufacturer
• Nominal RPM from manufacturer
• Inlet condition to lower stage compressor minimum or same as state of vapour in liquid separator
• Volume flow based on saturated condition at inlet
Oil separation and cooling
Oil will often, but not always be used during the compression process. Most screw compressors on the market
are oil injected, as oil free screw compressors are quite expensive due to an extra timing gear required, small
particles can cause complications and only necessary in certain applications where an oil free compression is
desired. The application in this project uses an oil injected screw compressor.
Oil serves a couple of purposes during the compression process. Oil acts as a sealant between rotor profiles,
to hinder the refrigerant from escaping the compression volume. Oil also acts as cooling for the compressor and
lubrication to minimize wear on the mechanical parts, these compressors are oil ﬂooded. After the compression
process the oil has to be separated and removed from the refrigerant. This is done in special oil separators which
are designed for the purpose.
Figure 2.11: Principle of oil separattion and return
30
==PAGE:46==
It is almost always desirable to prevent the refrigerant from entering the rest of the system and return it to
the compressor. However, small amounts of oil will still be carried along with the vapour into the system.
It is therefore important to design the system to make sure the oil returns to the compressor from a liquid
accumulator. Depending on the solubility and properties of the oil as well as refrigerant. Three distinct cases
can be described:
a) The oil is not soluble in the refrigerant and it has larger density than the refrigerant liquid.
b) The oil is not soluble in the refrigerant and it has lower density than the refrigerant liquid.
c) The oil is soluble in the refrigerant liquid.
Case a) will make the collect on the bottom where it will be easy to collect. Case b) will make the oil flow on
top. For case b) no separation will occur between the refrigerant liquid and oil.
Modelling the oil separation and cooling
For this application it will be assumed that the oil is totally separated from the refrigerant vapour after compres-
sion, cooled, returned and injected back into the refrigerant at inlet.
To model the oil cooling and state of the refrigerant at compressor discharge, including oil cooling, control
volumes are established around the complete compressor-oil separation system and the compressor itself. En-
ergy balance equations are established for these control volumes, given a constant volume flow of oil and inlet
temperature of the oil at compressor intake.

The compressor itself is treated as a process, with no accumulation of heat anywhere in the control volume.

isentropic efficiency and the work required for the oil is calculated as a pump. The oil and refrigerant is then
assumed to have a common temperature after compression work, and all of the oil is assumed to be removed
from the hot refrigerant vapour. The outlet state of refrigerant and the removed heat due to oil cooling can

then be estimated from the requirement of the control volume equilibrium and the outgoing energy flow from
the control volume.
Oil cooling assumptions
• Constant inlet temperature of oil to refrigerant at compressor inlet
• Constant volume flow of oil
• Common temperature of oil and refrigerant at compressor outlet
• Oil calculated as pump, work equates to pressure change
• Constant pump efficiency
• No heat loss to surroundings, all excess heat absorbed by oil
• Mass of water in oil cooler assumed to be constant
Heat exchangers
Many different types of heat exchangers are available for refrigeration and heat pump applications. The type
to use depend largely on the application and the mediums used for heat transfer. The mechanisms of heat
transfer in a heat exchanger is primarily governed by convection and conduction from a hot to a cold medium
separated by a wall. The rate of heat transfer is governed by the volume flow of the mediums, the temperature
gradient between the mediums, the convection and conduction coefficients and the area of heat transfer. When
the convection coefficient is low on one side, for example with an air/liquid evaporator, the heat transfer area as
well as flow on the air side can be increased to proportionally affect the rate of heat transfer up to a certain limit
with diminishing returns regarding the material used and possible flow.
The characteristics of the flows have a large influence on the convection coefficients, so naturally turbulence
theory plays a factor when considering the design of the heat exchanger. It is generally desired to make compact
heat exchangers to reduce amount of material used as well as practicality and ease of installation.
There are different approaches to model a heat exchanger, depending on the assumptions taken and detail
wantedinthemodel. ThemostcommonlyusedistheUA-methodwitheitheranarithmeticorlogarithmictem-
perature difference. The U-value signifies the collective average heat transfer coefficient of the heat exchanger,
and A signifies the heat transfer area. The overall instantaneous rate of heat transfer can be expressed by
ÛQ = UA · ∆Tlmtd
where,
∆Tlmtd = θ1 −θ2
ln θ1

θ2 = Thot,out −Tcold,out
A typical U-value for the type of heat exchanger used can be assumed for steady state operations, along with
area and temperature difference, to dimension the heat exchanger for the desired heat transfer rate. In reality
32
==PAGE:48==
things are more complicated. The overall heat transfer coefficient, the U-value, varies greatly along the flow di-
rection of the heat exchanging mediums, especially during phase changes on the refrigerant side. Correlations
will be necessary to precisely estimate the heat transfer coefficient at specific points locally along the flow direc-
tion. With the heat transfer area established from dimensioning of the heat exchanger, the heat transfer rate will
be determined by the flow and temperature difference of the mediums. Iterative computations will be required
to find a solution for the stated equation systems, depending on assumptions taken.
TheUA-methodofcalculatingheattransfer, doesn’tcaptureanytemporalthermodynamicdelaytheremight
be, from the heating of materials and volume of refrigerant in the heat exchanger. To capture the delay caused
by heat captured in mass, a more general expression of heat transfer, which account for a volume with a capacity
for containing heat can be used

The case shown in the figure is obviously the case of a hot refrigerant rejecting heat to a colder heat carrying
medium, such as water, in this case. For the evaporator the reverse case would be true, where the ’hot’ water
will be rejecting heat to the colder refrigerant, undergoing an evaporation process, absorbing the necessary heat
from the water, into latent heat during evaporation.
Each incremental volume is denoted with the subscript of i, where in the case shown, there are 10 incremental
sub-volumes each for the refrigerant, wall and water part, meaning 30 incremental sub-volumes in total for one
heat exchanger. Depending on the formulation of heat transfer used, the expressions in the system of equations
in 2.3 or 2.4, the discretisation will be approached in one of two ways.
System of differential equations for each sub-volume
Using the system of equations in 2.3,

described with an integral equation. Going in the direction of flow, the state of refrigerant in one volume i will
be dependent on the flow of energy from the preceding volume i −1, and the outgoing flow to the subsequent
volumei +1. In addition, there will be a heat loss to the wall of the same volume, depending on the temperature
difference, area of wall in the current volume i and convection coefficient. The expression for state change of

Linearising temperature drops/rises
A different approach to modelling the transferred heat in a heat
exchanger, can be found in assuming a linear temperature drop along the flow direction of the heat exchanger.
This approach was necessary, as the former formulation with differential equations for each single sub-volume
introduced numerical errors, which will be treated in more detail during verification in chapter 4.
Linearising the temperatures introduces simplification of the model to a degree, with far fewer differential
equations. The sub-volumes of the heat exchangers are still in use, as the convection coefficients need to be
estimated discretely along the flow direction of the heat exchanger. But the state in each sub-volume is assumed
to be defined linearly from the inlet to the outlet of the flow.

The wall as a temperature sink is neglected, as the heat transfer in each incremental step, is assumed to be
governed by the local U-value, where the conduction coefficient of the wall doesn’t contribute as much as the
convection coefficients, and the wall thickness is negligible. The conduction of the wall is included in the U-
value, where the wall temperature is assumed to be the local average medium temperature between the hot and
the cold flow. Using this method, the heat transfer can be estimated incrementally along the flow direction and
summed up. The summed heat transfer is then used to describe the temporal state change at the outlet of the
heat exchanger by the expression in Equation 2.4.
Assumptions for the heat exchangers
• Temperature assumed to be linear from inlet to outlet
• Wall neglected as temporal delay for heat transfer
