==PAGE:51==
• Convection coefficients estimated incrementally along the heat exchanger with correlations from litera-
ture
• State of medium assumed to be uniform in one sub-volume
• Heat transfer estimated incrementally with local U-value and arithmetic temperature difference
Flooded evaporator PHE
This application used a flooded PHE evaporator for ammonia evaporation, with sea water as heat source for
evaporation. A flooded evaporator functions in conjunction with a vessel containing a mixture of liquid and
vapour, also called a liquid separator or receiver. A flooded evaporator functions by partly evaporating the re-
frigerant, so a mixture of liquid and vapour exits from it, contrary to a DX (Dirext Expansion) evaporator which
is normally used in smaller applications. With large industrial applications, flooded evaporators are normally
preferred as they are easier to control and the heat transfer coefficient is higher towards the outlet.
(a) Plate Heat Exchanger configuration
Evaporator PHE
Liquid Separator
Refrigant PHE loop
From Intercooler
To compressor
Liquid
Vapour
(b) Evaporation loop
Figure 2.14: Flooded Evaporator configuration
The evaporator design itself is a plate heat exchanger, where the refrigerant and water flow in interchanging
channels created by the space between the plates. The surface of the plates themselves are arranged in different
ways, to create turbulence in the flows and increase the heat transfer coefficient of convection on either side of
the plate.
A literature survey was done, to find a fitting correlation for ammonia evaporation in plate heat exchangers.
Plate heat exchangers have just recently started seeing more widespread use, due to their compactness and small
internal volume, which results in low refrigeration charges. The overall heat transfer coefficient for these types
of heat exchangers are quite high. Since ammonia is corrosive in contact with copper, semi-welded types have to
be used, instead of the more common brazed plate heat exchangers, where copper is used for the brazing.
Since the introduction of plate heat exchangers have been relatively recent, there’s not as much experimental
work available for the different configurations of refrigerants and plate corrugation patterns as for other, older
type of heat exchangers. Several teams have however done more recent experimental work, since the 00’s, to
obtain data points on which correlations could be established.
Eldeeb et al. did a large literature survey, where they summarized the current state of affairs of research into
correlations for heat transfer and pressure drop for evaporation and condensation in plate heat exchangers [17].
This survey paper contains an overview and review of current developed correlations and references to the re-
search papers.
36
==PAGE:52==
Evaporation heat transfer is mainly the result of nucleate boiling and forced convection, each contributing to
the heat transfer coefficient. It is thus necessary to know the mass flux, which is obtained from the dimensions
and flow, as well as the local saturation quality along the flow direction. Naturally a wide range of experiments
covering a wide range of conditions for the evaporation is necessary to establish satisfactory correlations with
minimum deviation from observed results. Different experiments have determined different primary mecha-
nisms of boiling as determining the heat flux. Forced convective boiling has been associated with flooded evap-
orators with high mass fluxes, while nucleate boiling is associated with DX evaporators at low mass fluxes and
high heat flux. Many factors affect the heat transfer coefficient and Eldeeb et al. summarized the different results
and conclusions from several research teams in section 2 of their survey paper [17].
Amalfi et al. collected of most of the current work and made an experimental database containing the data
points from earlier experiments [18] and established a new correlation from the collected data points. After-
wards they compared the different predictor methods including their new correlation [19], with good results
for the new predictor. the correlation is valid on a wide range of refrigerants and operating conditions.
The obtained correlation was simplified and utilized for this project, to estimate a convection coefficient for
the refrigerant flow boiling in the evaporator.
Likewise, a correlation for single phase fluid heat transfer was found in a research paper by Talal M. Abou et
al. [20]. The correlation was developed from experimental data and research on corrugated plate heat exchang-
ers, as they were being used widely for heat recuperation and energy savings purposes, due to ease of installation
and adaptability. The correlation is valid for a wide range of operations for single phase fluids, and was adapted
for estimation of brine liquids (sea water salinity).

• Saturated liquid at inlet flow to evaporator:

Liquid Separator
The liquid separator has the function of separating the vapour and liquid. The liquid separator is essentially
a receiver for the refrigerant flow from the inter-cooling vessel, regulated by a low pressure ﬂoat valve which in
turn is governed by the liquid level in the separator.
The state in the liquid separator is a mixture of liquid and vapour, where the saturated liquid in the bottom
of the vessel is led into the plate heat exchanger and re-enters the vessel as partially evaporated refrigerant. The
37
==PAGE:53==
evaporation loop can either be naturally driven by convection (gravity driven) due to the heated vapour and
liquid from evaporation or the refrigeration flow can be driven by a pump.
The saturated vapour gasses in the top of the vessel are drawn into the suction line of the compressor. This
further increases the flow of refrigerant, as the density and thus the pressure of the vapour in the vessel decreases.

The approach to model the change of pressure in the vessel was to make as few assumptions as possible and
allow for the pressure and the liquid level to change. With the dimensions of the vessel given the total volume
and the volume of liquid and vapour can be calculated, as well as a filling volume ratio stated from a ratio of
liquid to vapour. Equations are set up for the flows of gas and liquid in and out of the vessel.

mixture the density can be calculated and the volume of liquid found. With a volume balance for the vessel, the

Intercooling vessel
The intercooler largely serves similar function as the liquid separator and acts as a receiver for the refrigerant
at the intermediary pressure. After having been partially desuperheated, superheated vapour enters from the
low pressure stage compressor. A condensation pressure valve controls the pressure drop of refrigerant from
the higher condensation pressure, while the low pressure float valve controls the flow of refrigerant to the lower
evaporation pressure. At the same time there is a flow of vapour from the low stage compressor, while the high
stage compressor controls the flow of gas for condensation.

Condenser PHE
For the condenser, a plate heat exchanger configuration is used to transfer heat to the heat sink (district heat wa-
ter) from the refrigerant during the condensation phase. The condenser serves the obvious task of transferring
the majority of heat to the heat sink, by giving off its latent heat in the phase change during condensation, while
also serving as ’resetting’ the refrigerant to liquid for the heat absorption during evaporation. Depending on
the size of the system and practicality, the condenser can be configured as transferring all of the heat given off to
the heat sink, including desuperheating and subcooling, or configured as separate heat exchangers covering only
desuperheating, condensing and subcooling respectively. Most of the heat absorbed and added to the system
from the evaporation and compressor is rejected during condensation to the heat sink. For the configuration in
this project, the PHE condenser primarily handles the condensation phase only, while the desuperheating and
subcooling is handled separately by other heat exchangers.
40
==PAGE:56==
From the refrigerant side, the super heated vapour should be just entering as saturated vapour to the phase
change from vapour to liquid, and at the outlet the state would be about fully saturated liquid. The heat sink
side consists of water for distributing heat to the district heat network. In principle the construction of the
condenser is quite similar to the evaporator as a plate heat exchanger. Corrugated angled plates to create turbu-
lence and interchanging channels of water and refrigerant to increase heat exchange coefficients for the fluids.
The correlation for local convection coefficient is expressed differently though, as condensation follow different
mechanics than boiling in the evaporator.
A literature survey was done, to find suitable correlations for condensation in a plate heat exchanger. For
plate heat exchangers there has not been nearly as many experiments for finding a correlation for ammonia con-
densation as for ammonia boiling. Eldeeb et al. [17] summarized several research papers on condensation for
plate heat exchangers, in the same manner as for evaporation. Condensation mechanics in plate heat exchangers
are recognized to mainly be determined to be gravity-controlled or shear-controlled. Nusselt was the first to
come up with a correlation, based on his theoretical study of laminar condensation over a cooled metal surface
[21]. Since then other correlations have been developed, with Shah [22] developing a correlation for conden-
sation in horizontal, vertical and inclined pipes, which is widely used, due perhaps to being developed from a
wide range of data. A better correlation for the special geometrical considerations for plate heat exchangers is
desired though. A number of research teams have conducted experimental work, to determine which factors to
consider for developing a correlation.
Longo et al. [23] collected most of this experimental work, to develop a new correlation for predicting the
heat exchange convection coefficient for condensation in a plate heat exchanger. This new predictor was com-
pared to the other correlations and was found to perform greatly for a wide range of refrigerants. this correlation
was adapted and utilized in this project.
Likewise the single-phase correlation for the water side was re-utilized from the single-phase correlation for
brine from the evaporator, to predict the convection coefficient for the heat sink.

Desuperheater & Subcooler SHT
The desuperheaters and subcoolers of the heat pump system are constructed in the shell & tube configuration
(SHT), where the refrigerant flows inside bundles of tubes arranged inside a shell, where water flows on the out-
side of the tubes. SHT heat exchangers can be arranged in a multitude of ways, with baffles to induce cross flow
41
==PAGE:57==
and turbulence, several passes through, parallel and counter flow, different tube cross section layouts. Special
consideration regarding the material used for the heat exchanger is needed, when using ammonia as refriger-
ant, as ammonia is corrosive to zinc, copper and brass. Maintaining the purity of the ammonia as refrigerant is
critical as well, as impurities can increase the corrosion risk.
(a) Shell & Tube
(b) Straight tube one pass tube side
(c) Straight tube two pass tube side
(d) U-Tube
Figure 2.17: Shell & Tube configurations
For the desuperheaters, the refrigerants enter in state of superheated vapour from the outlet of the compres-
sor, as such they serve multiple functions for the system as a whole. A primary function obviously being the
transfer of heat to the heat sink, and desuperheating the refrigerant vapour till the point of entering conden-
sation. Each heat pump is configured as a two stage system, meaning that the compression is conducted over
two stages, with intercooling and desuperheating the hot gas at the intermediary pressure stage. This serves to
reduce compressor load, and increases the overall coefficient of performance for the system as a whole.
For the shell & tube configuration, a literature survey was done to find suitable correlations for predicting the
heat transfer coefficient along the flow direction and implement them in the system model. For the refrigerant
side a general heat transfer correlation was easily obtained, as the heat transfer for a flow inside a tube has been
extensively studied. There is a multitude of correlations to choose from, and the one chosen for this application
was the Colburn analogy, which briefly states h = 1

Estimating the heat transfer coefficient for the flow inside the tube was thus mainly a matter of calculating
the mass flux through one tube, which comes down to dimensioning and mass flow.
Estimating the heat transfer coefficient on the outside of the tube banks is a slightly more complicated affair.
Granryd, chapter 8 [15] provides a helpful method. Granryd references a book on heat transfer by Holman
[24], where work by Grimison [25] is used. The relation Nu = C · Ren · Pr
1
3 is given, where the constant C
and exponent n are given by table values shown in Granryd for staggered and in-line tube arrangements.
(a) In line tube arrangement
(b) Staggered tube arrangement
Figure 2.18: Tube arrangements cross flow
Furthermore, Granryd references Pierre [26] recommending a staggered tube arrangement with a selection
of ratios Sn/d and Sp/d for finding the table values C and n.
The dimensionless Nusselt number can thus be calculated from this relation, and the overall heat transfer
coefficient estimated for the surface of the tube banks in a cross flow configuration shell & tube heat exchanger.
It should be kept in mind, that more complex correlations for calculating heat transfer coefficient might be avail-
able in literature, but for the sake of simplicity, this relation was chosen.

Valves
(a) Electronic expansion valve
(b) Low pressure float valve
Figure 2.19: Different types of valves
Several different types of valves are necessary for the function of the heat pump. Most critical of these are the
valves controlling the expansion of the refrigerant in the basic concept of the heat pump. The valve serves the
function of reducing the pressure from the high condensation pressure to the lower evaporation pressure by ge-
ometrical constriction and expansion or simply, flow control. As the refrigerant is in a state near phase change,
the reduction in pressure will reduce the temperature as well, for the evaporation process to absorb heat from
the heat source. Many different types of valve concepts exist controlled by various means, manually, thermo-
statically, automatically or electronically. Which type to choose depends entirely on the type of operation and
complexity of the heat pump. Granryd, chapter 10 [15] gives a comprehensive overview of the different types.
Assumptions for valves
• Massflow assumed to be linearly dependent on valve opening
• Pressure differential over valve influences massflow
• ’Imaginary’ valve characteristic dimensioned to operating conditions, set constant
Low stage - Low pressure ﬂoat valve
The two stage ammonia heat pump uses a flooded evaporator with a liquid separator, with an intercooling
vessel at an intermediary pressure. As such a low pressure float valve is used to control the level of liquid in the
liquid separator, by regulating the flow of refrigerant from the intercooling vessel. As such the drop in pressure
is actuated by the flow constriction of the valve and the expansion of refrigerant in the liquid separator vessel,
rather than being controlled by the superheat after a DX evaporator, as is normally the case with an expansion
valve.
Modelling the low pressure float valve requires an equation to express the change in flow according to chang-
ing state in operation variable. As such it was a desire in this approach to keep the valve model relatively simple.
44
==PAGE:60==
A relative simple relation for a valve with a certain characteristic and varying opening degree can be expressed as
Ûm = ρ · Z · Kv ·

∆P
(2.13)
High stage - Condensation pressure
A valve controlling the condensation pressure is necessary as well for the function of the heat pump, as the
condensation pressure controls the temperature during the phase change as well, which is primarily driving the
heat transfer rate at steady flow and operation. The condensation pressure valve is in this case controlled by a
sensor measuring the liquid/vapour saturation out of the condenser and regulation the constriction accordingly
tomaintainthestateofsaturatedliquidoutofthecondenser. Assuch, theliquidlevelandintermediarypressure
in the intercooling vessel is not directly controlled, and is free to vary according to changing states and flows in
the rest of the system, though the system will be dimensioned and charged with refrigerant to operate with an
optimal intermediary pressure for the operation of the two compressor stages.
To model the condensation pressure valve control, a simple approach was used, where the control of the
pressure was directly influenced by a PI-control regulator, using the saturated liquid enthalpy at the outlet of
the condenser as a control variable. This solution was chosen as the mass-flow is primarily influenced by the
compressor, and as the mass-flow varies greatly during ramp-up of compressor revolutions, causing instability
of condensation pressure, the condensation pressure was assumed to be linearly and singularly dependent on
an opening degree operating within a defined minimum and maximum pressure area, disregarding pressure
differential over the valve and valve characteristic
Ûm = openvalve
100
· (Pmax −Pmin) + Pmin
(2.14)
Flow pumps
In a large heat pump system, a number of circulation pumps might be required to maintain high enough pres-
sure and flow at various points in the system, especially on the heat sink side, where district water is distributed
to the different heat exchangers for absorbing the rejected heat from the heat pump, as well as on the heat source
side for regulating the flow of sea water to the evaporator. For a model able to handle various modes of oper-
ations outside of steady state, it is important to model the flow pumps with the ability to regulate their flow
according to a controlling state variable. In most cases the flow pumps are controlled by an outlet temperature
from the heat exchangers. The heat source flow pumps are regulated to maintain a certain temperature drop
across the evaporators. If the temperature drop is too high the flow is increased to reduce the risk of freezing the
seawater.
Assumptions for the auxiliary pumps
• Incompressible fluid
• Work in pumps based on pressure differential
• Constant pump efficiency ηpump = 0.9
• Pressure differential constant and assumed
45
==PAGE:61==
2.3 Mathematical model
To be able to simulate the heat pump during start-up, the components have been expressed mathematically. As
the start-up of the heat pump is starting from off design, and the state changes during start-up vary a great deal,
it was deemed necessary to express a relatively complex model, with the capability of operating in a great range
of changing states. This requires a detailed expression to simulate the required thermodynamic phenomena.
Compressor modelling
To express the compressor mathematically, requires finding a method for expressing the isentropic and volu-
metric efficiency, for the purpose of estimating the work and refrigerant mass moved by the compressor.
Isentropic efficiency
Depending on the type of compressor, the isentropic efficiency can be expressed in variety of ways. For steady
state simulations, it is at times satisfactory to assume a constant isentropic efficiency, but as the operating con-
ditions, which the compressor work in, and the pressure difference it has to overcome varies, the efficiency of
the compressor varies as well. Correlations in the form of compressor polynomials are sometimes supplied by
the compressor manufacturer, based on standardised testing conditions for compressors. No such is available
from GEA, for the compressors used in this project. Granryd [15, Chapter 7] provides a general expression for
the rotary screw compressor, based on the pressure differential, the in-built volumetric compression and the
polytropic exponent at compressor inlet. The formulation is seen in Equation 2.15.
ηopt =
(P1/P2)(k−1)/k −1
π(k−1)/k

where ηopt is the ideal isentropic efficiency of the compressor, πi = νk

tio, νi is the volumetric compression ratio of the compressor k is the polytropic exponent at compressor inlet,
and P1 and P2 are the compressor inlet and outlet pressure, respectively. ηis represents the actual isentropic
efficiency of the compressor, while ηmax is then the assumed maximal efficiency of the compressor working at
optimal conditions.
Volumetric efficiency
The volumetric efficiency is used to estimate the flow of refrigerant through the compressor. The compressor
has a geometric swept volume, expressing the volume of refrigerant than be theoretically forced through the
compressor per revolution. But as no compressor is perfect, even as efforts are made to reduce leakage, there will
be a slight flow back through the compressor of refrigerant. The volumetric efficiency expresses this relationship
between the theoretical volume flow and the actual volume flow. Normally, the relationship of the volumetric
efficiency can be expressed and is supplied via a correlation expressed as a polynomial. But as this isn’t supplied
from GEA, another relation from [16], is used, expressed in Equation 2.16
ηv = 0.86
for

Oil cooling
In some cases, when establishing work equations for the compressor, simpler relations can assume a heat loss
factor for the compressor, which defines the mechanical work that is lost as heat, if the compressor work is not
assumed an adiabatic process that is. In this case, as the compressor is oil cooled, and the heat absorbed by the
oil is rejected to the district heating network, the constituting equations involves a number of extras steps to
take into account. A simple heat loss factor can’t be assumed, as the heat absorbed by the oil, depends on the
work which the compressor does under the varying operation.
A control volume around the collective compressor and oil cooling process needs to be established, to check
the in- and outflow of energies, and establish the equations for the common temperatures of oil and refrigerant
after the compression process.
Compressor inlet condition
As the isentropic efficiency describes the relation between an isentropic reversible process and the actual pro-
cess, the entropy at inlet is used to establish the enthalpy at the outlet of the reversible process, where no work
is lost.

Vessel modelling
The vessel model is intended to simulate the change of liquid level, pressure and temperatures of the vapour, as
well as the evaporation of liquid due to superheated vapour.
The system of equations depend on three integral equations. Two for the change in mass of liquid and gas,
and one for the change in energy of gas. The state of liquid is assumed to be pure liquid and the state of vapour
48
==PAGE:64==
varies between pure and superheated vapour. There is assumed to be no variable state between pure liquid and
vapour.
The time dependent change of mass for both liquid and vapour is found based on the mass flows. A volume
balance is established and the volumes are found. Based on an assumption of pure liquid, the liquid level and
specific volume for the vapour in the vessel is calculated. To establish the pressure, an integral equation for the
change in energy of the vapour in the vessel is established, based on the enthalpies of the inlet and outlet flows
in the liquid separator.
Volumes of liquid and vapour
The total volume of the vessel is found from the diameter and length, which is supplied by the budget quo-
tation in Appendix C. Furthermore, the volume of liquid and vapour is established from a filling factor f
Vtotal = π · D2
4 · L
Vliq = Vtotal · f
Vgas = Vtotal · (1 −f )
(2.25)

General heat transfer
Describing heat transfer theoretically can be done in a number of ways, varying according to which heat transfer
mechanic is driving the heat transfer, convection, conduction or radiation. Radiation does not have strong
influence on the heat transfer described for this model, and will thus be neglected.
The heat transfer mechanics in a vapour compression heat pump are for a large part convective and conduc-
tive. Two fluids in either gas or liquid phase are flowing through a heat exchanger, separated by a wall, through
which conductive heat transfer will take place. The convective heat transfer physics of the fluids transfers heat
from hot fluid to wall, and from wall to cold fluid. Often for a heat exchanger, an overall UA-value will be used,
the U-value describing the overall heat transfer coefficient for the convective and conductive heat transfer, while
the A value describes the total heat transfer area of the heat exchanger. This value times the mean temperature
difference will give the heat transfer.
General energy balance
The heat transfer in a heat exchanger can be seen as control volume, where the flow of energy in the hot
and cold side must stay equal, determining the outlet conditions of either as a consequence of the 1st law of
thermodynamics stating that energy is never lost,
ÛQ = Ûm · cp · (Tout −Tin)
ÛQ = Ûm · (hin −hout)
(2.31)
Convective heat transfer
50
==PAGE:66==
Convective heat transfer states the heat transfer mechanics that come into play, as a fluid in motion transfers
the heat contained in its molecules to another body. Movement and the properties of the fluid play a key role in
the rate of heat transfer, as it determines the coefficient α. Turbulence plays a large part, as it increases the heat
transfer coefficient, and is thus often sought to be induced in the fluid in motion
ÛQ = α · A · (Tf luid −Twall)
(2.32)
Conductive heat transfer
Conductive heat transfer is the heat transfer in a solid body, where the energy is transferred between the
exchange and collision of the particles constituting the molecules of the body. It is largely dependent on the
properties of the body constituting the conductivity λw as well as the thickness δw. The uniformity of the body
also plays a part, important for insulating materials, but negligible in this application.
ÛQ = δw
λw
· A · (Tw,1 −Tw,2)
(2.33)
Overall heat transfer
Once the conductivity properties of the heat exchanger itself is determined and the convective properties of
the fluids exchanging heat are determined, an overall U-value for the heat transfer can be determined. With the
design of the heat exchanger known, giving the area of heat transfer, the heat transfer can be determined, with
a mean temperature difference. The mean temperature difference can be determined logarithmically along the
the temperature curves or simply arithmetically. This formulation is also called the UA-method.
ÛQ = UA · ∆Tlmtd
where,
1
U = 1
α1
+ δw
λw
+ 1
α2
(2.34)
Speciﬁc heat transfer
The aforementioned equations are suitable for steady state calculations, where the operation is assumed con-
stant and conditions are stable. When including thermodynamic effects over shorter temporal scales, where an
energy system undergoes a wide range of changing conditions, the energy equations have to capture those tem-
poral changes, with the added buffer of sub-control volumes storing energy, causing a delay in state changes.
This is facilitated by integrating over differential equations.

The equation for the refrigerant side, needs to take into account phase changes in the refrigerant, and as such
uses the state of enthalpy for the in and out flow to the volume, but also uses the temperature of the body of
fluid for the heat transfer to the wall.

Dimensioning
For the correlations, it is necessary to establish flow characteristics, where geometry of the heat exchangers play
a large role. For the project, it is thus necessary to establish dimensions. For the project to be able to simulate as
closetotherealheatpumpsthemselves, somegivendataisnecessary. Inthecaseofinsufficientdata, assumptions
have to be made. Some of the data is available from the budget quotation of GEA in Appendix C, such as
materials and dimensions, other more detailed informations such as heat exchangers sizes, corrugation, number
of tubes etc. have been assumed and adjusted to make the heat pump perform as wanted during steady state
and start-up.
Plate heat exchanger dimensions
The plate heat exchanger is arranged as a number of parallel plates with gaps in between, forming interchanging
channels where refrigerant and water flow separately, exchanging heat through the plate wall. The plate heat
exchanger dimensioning has been simplified in this case, as the detailed dimensioning of heat exchanger area
profiles require extra consideration for corrugation, effective heat transfer area and geometrical detail. But for
sake of complicity in this project, the walls are treated geometrically as flat plates, and an assumed corrugation
angle is given for convection correlations.
Area and number of channels
The total heat transfer area is calculated from the width and height of the plate, as well as the number of
plates, while the number of channels for each side is half the number of plates.
Ahx = Hplate · Wplate · nplate
nch = nplate
2
(2.44)
Flow characteristic in a channel
The flow characteristic in a channel is determined by the following set of equations. The cross sectional
area Af ch is determined, as well as the circumference Cf ch of a flow channel. The hydraulic diameter of a
flow channel Dh is determined for use in the correlation, while the total cross sectional area Af low of flow is
determined, from which the mass flux G is determined for use in the correlation. As the water and refrigerant
54
==PAGE:70==
side is identical in geometry, the general set of equations are formulated below.

Shell & Tube dimensions
For the shell & tube heat exchanger, the flow characteristics are determined differently for the refrigerant and
water side. The refrigerant flows inside the tubes, while the water flows shell-side around the tubes.
Total volume
In the budget quotation, the approximate volume of refrigerant and water in the heat exchanger is given. But
asthecorrelationswhichareusedinthemodelcan’tbeassumedtobeasormoreprecisethanthemanufacturers,
the volumes from data are only used to establish relations for the total volume, which is adjusted slightly with
the number of tubes in use.
Vrat = Vw,data
Vref,data

(2.46)
Number of tubes
For quick adjustment of the number of tubes, the tubes were assumed to be arranged in columns of two,
where the second column has one extra tube from the first column, as the tubes are assumed to be in a staggered
arrangement. The number of rows in the first line is assumed, and the number of columns is then assumed
equal to number of rows, with the addition of one extra column for the structure to be uniform.
The outer diameter of the tubes is assumed and adjustable, which with an assumed outer and inner diameter
ratio gives the inner diameter.
ntubes = (2 · nrows + 1) · nrows + nrows

Convection Correlations
The following correlations have been established in literature, by extensive research and testing of the flow prop-
erties and characteristics of different fluids. The correlations are in some cases based on a massive array of data
points. Further research is still being done, as new heat exchanger designs and optimisations are done. As far
as the writer of this project knows, these correlations present the newest available research which collects data
points from past experiments to establish new correlations that cover a wider range of use cases for the different
refrigerants.
For all the correlations, it is necessary to supply information about the flow characteristics, such as hydraulic
diameter Dh and mass flux G, along with the state of the fluid for which a coefficient has to be found, since prop-
erties of the fluid are necessary to establish dimensionless numbers describing the fluid characteristics, which all
of the correlations are dependent on. A different quantity, that has to be established is the heat flux q′′, which
is the heat transferred per area. The correlations have to be iterated upon, as the heat flux is dependent on the
heat transfer coefficients, properties of the fluid as well as the temperature differences, which in turn are de-
pendent on the heat flux. This is handled by supplying an initial guess value for the heat flux, from which the
computational model is able to find a solution.
Evaporator PHE correlation
The correlation for for evaporation of ammonia in a plate heat exchanger was obtained from research done by
Amalfi et al. [19], the contents of which can be studied in further detail from the supplied research paper.

2.4 District heat network modelling
The heat added to the inflow of cold district heating water has to be modelled separately, to estimate the heating
of the water from several separate smaller streams joining up with the main flow, to split and join again. The
principal diagram of the ehat exchanger netowrk can be seen in Figure 2.20.
60
==PAGE:76==
Subcooler HP1
Subcooler HP2
Desuperheater LP1
Desuperheater LP2
Desuperheater HP1
Condenser HP1
Condenser HP2
Oilcooler HP2
Oilcooler HP1
Oilcooler LP1
Oilcooler LP2
Desuperheater HP2
Figure 2.20: SVAF District Water heat exchanger network
The first group of heat exchanger each heat up a smaller stream of water, for them to join up with the main
flow, going through the HP1 condenser, before splitting up again for separate heat exchangers for the last heat-
ing.
1st group of heat exchangers
To handle the modelling of the network, it is necessary to establish mass and energy balances at the various
points along the interconnected heat exchangers. Given the various massflows to each heat exchanger, and the
main district heating water flow from the main flow pump, the first group of heat exchangers consists of:
• Subcooler HP1
• Subcooler HP2
• Desuperheater LP1
• Desuperheater LP2
The collective massflows for these heat exchangers are subtracted from the main flow. The flow for the con-
denser is equal to the main flow, as all the streams are joined again, before entering
Ûmf low,1 = Ûmmain −  Ûmsubco,HP1 + Ûmsubco,H P2 + Ûmdesup,LP1 + Ûmdesup,LP2

Ûmcond,HP1 = Ûmmain
(2.63)

main flow again, before the HP1 condenser by an energy balance at the joint, to find the state at the condenser
inlet. With the state of the main flow being equal to the state at the main water inlet and the inlet flow to the
condenser being equal to the total main flow, we can find the inlet state hcond,H P1,in by the following set of

2.5 Control strategy for system & components
As the aim of the model is to be able to simulate a wide range of thermodynamic conditions outside of steady
state, the most severe being start-up of the heat pump, where the states change drastically from initial condi-
tions, it is necessary to implement a control scheme, to make sure that the system is operable, and the system
of equations doesn’t diverge and deviate from realistic physical phenomena. As the flows, heat transfer, tem-
peratures and state of refrigerant change during start-up, valves, pumps, compressor RPM and actuators are
changed according to an input signal for the opening degree/capacity. Different methods and philosophies
for controllers have been developed over the years, from the simple manual human error corrections, over au-
tomatically controlled error correction schemes to complicated black-box correlation methods, depending on
system complexity and target of control. This project will go into the deeper intricacies of the many different
approaches to control schemes, as it is not within the scope to cover this field extensively.
The aim of every control scheme, is for one or several quantifiable and measurable states to stay constant or
within a certain limit of operation. As such, a setpoint that the measurable state should stay within a certain
threshold of is defined, which the measured state is compared to, and from said comparison, an error signal is
detected. This error signal is used for correction, by requesting a state change elsewhere in the system, by varying
the capacity signal to a compressor or a pump, or changing the opening degree of an actuator controlled valve,
as an example. Often the measurable state, will be pressure or temperature, but other controller signals are of
course possible, as anything that can be measured and have a significant enough correlation between the process
variable and the controllable and variable component.
Thisprocess ismost oftencontrolled bya smallcompactcomputer, aPLC(ProgrammableLogicController),
which contains a programmable environment with logic gates for controlling output signals based on input
signals.
PI controls
A simple controller concept is the proportional controller, which consists of only the P block in Figure 2.21.
The proportional controller, simply put, corrects the error between the process variable (PV) and the setpoint
63
==PAGE:79==
(SP) by multiplying a gain Kp on the error for the controller output, the manipulable variable (MV). This
is necessary, as the the manipulable variable is often in a completely different scale than the process variable.
As is the case of a capacity signal for revolutions in a compressor, controlled by a temperature setpoint in the
process. If the temperature of water is too low at a certain point in the district heating network, the compressor
will increase its revolutions to increase the heat rejection rate of the heat pump, heating the water flow to the
required temperature. The expression for the proportional controller is formulated as
e(t) = SP −PV
MV(t) = MVstart + P
P = Kp · e(t)
(2.70)
The MVstart is a constant, from which the controller starts, to offset the error jump, which the process vari-
able gives to the manipulable variable, as the controller kicks in.
The integral part of the PI-controller, the I block in Figure 2.21, is introduced to eliminate the Steady State
Error (SSE), which is an offset introduced by the proportional controller being dependent on a measurable
error for the controller to function. This introduces a constant error, once steady state is reached, which is
proportional to the gain of the error.
As the proportional controller only reacts to the current error at time t, the history of the error need to be
taken into account, which is done with an integral part, that sums the error over time, using it to negate SSE.
I = Ki
Ti
∫
e(t)dt
(2.71)
where Ki is the gain for the integral part (equal to Kp in this case) and Ti is the reset time, which can be used
to adjust the weight of the gain, controlling how large of an effect the integral part of the controller has, and in
essence how ’quickly’ it corrects the offset error. The complete controller scheme is shown in Figure 2.21.
Figure 2.21: PI-controller scheme
64
==PAGE:80==
The complete formulation for the PI-controller is then
MV(t) = MVstart + Kp ·

e(t) + 1
Ti
∫
e(t)dt

(2.72)

Conditional controls for process variables
Duringascenariosuchasstart-upofthecompressor,someconditionswerenecessarytosetforthePI-controllers.
As the initial water temperatures in the district heating network would be low, the initial error on the control
signals for the compressors and the feed pump would would be significant, as to cause errors and divergence in
the system of equations, or the integral part of the PI-controller would sum the error for a large portion of the
simulation, which the controller would not be able to revert from.
As such, to continue with the example from start-up, a limit would be set on the process variable used to
control the said component. If the initial temperature of water outlet was TDH,out = 50°℃, and the outlet
temperature of water is used to control both the feed pump and the compressor capacity for heat pump 2, the
signal would be limited in such a way, as to not count as an error until a threshold was reached. In this case an
offset value of 2°℃. When the process variable, in this case the outlet temperature reaches this threshold value,
the controller is inactive as no error is detected.
2.6 Computational model
Description of the tool used for describing, handling, computing and simulating the mathematical system
model.
The interaction of the sections in the EES model interacting it laid out in figure 2.24. The sections are not
shown in order as they appear in the model. But as the EES program automatically orders the equations entered
for numerical solving, the order of equations doesn’t matter, as is the case for other programs, such as Matlab,
which executes written scripts in order, as an example. The key component of the model is the declaration of the
components. This section calls on most of the other sections of the model. The inlet/outlet states of each of the
components are stated here, as well as the governing geometrical dimensions, which can be used for adjusting
the heat transfer of the components. Each component calls a module, which in turn call other libraries for
calculating inner dimensions of the heat exchanger, discretises and calculate the heat transfer coefficients and
heat flux along the heat exchanger by calling the correlation in each discretised volume, sums the total heat
transferred and gives it as an output. Some of the components, such as the compressors contain calls to loaded
libraries.
The temporal changes expressed by integral equations, in the different key points of the heat pumps need
to be initialised with an initial start value, which is done with the values in the startup definitions. As the heat
pump undergoes changes and delivers heat in a large thermodynamic span, different components of the heat
pump need be controlled according to various setpoints. The heat pump components, such as compressors and
valves are controlled by PI-controllers, governed by conditional equations and setpoints.
67
==PAGE:83==
The water side absorbing heat from the heat pump, is governed by PI-controllers as well, one controller
governing the main flow of water, as well as controllers for each heat exchanger, adjusting the flow of water
through one single heat exchanger each, according to temperature setpoints. Energy balances for the different
points where water join and split are calculated in a block of four module calls, calculating four separate group
of heat exchangers, to find common temperatures, where the flows join up again.
From all this, main compressor work, heat delivered and auxiliary work is calculated and the total efficiency
for the system is found.
Defined functions in the EES model, for various purposes are used generally in the model, and at last integral
tables for gauging a various assortment of variables and to judge performance after a simulation run gathers all
the wanted variables from the various parts of the model.

Initialising the model for simulation
The computational model needs to be initialised before doing a simulation, to update initial guess values at
various points along the system, as the system of equations will otherwise diverge.
- To start with, the evaporation, intermediary and condenser pressures are set to constants, which is deter-
mined based on the starting conditions, while the controls for those states are turned off.
69
==PAGE:85==
- The capacity for the compressors and the main flow pump is set to a constant capacity/speed, but not
lower or higher than their minimum or maximum rating respectively.
- The outlet states for the water and refrigerant side of the heat exchangers are set to the initial conditions.
Either low temperature simulating startup, setpoint values to simulate starting from steady state or any-
thing in between if wanted
- In each heat exchanger module, the heat flux for each sub-volume qi, is set to the average heat flux qavr,
to initialise guess values, which once initialised are set to qi again and iterated a second time to gain a
more refined guess values for qi
EES model / libraries
• lmtd.lib - The lmtd procedure for determining the heat transfer based on the UA-lmtd method
• oilCompCool.lib - Procedure for determining oil cooling and outlet state after compressor and oil
cooler
• compressor.lib - Procedure containing the calculations for compressor efficiencies and mass flow esti-
mations

• evaporatorPHE.lib-Refrigerantside,evaporatorplateheatexchangercorrelationdependentonvapour

• singlePhasePHE.lib - Water side, condenser plate heat exchanger for standard water
• ShellTube_shellgas.lib - Shell & Tube, shellside for fluid in gas phase
• ShellTube_shellliq.lib - Shell & Tube, shellside for fluid in liquid phase
• ShellTube_tubegas.lib - Shell & Tube, inside tubes for fluid in gas phase
• ShellTube_tubeliq.lib - Shell & Tube, inside tubes for fluid in liquid phase
Geometrical information - Modules for calculating the dimensions for each heat exchanger
• evaporator_LP1-2.lib - The module containing information about assumed plate height, length and
thickness of one plate in the evaporator, calculating the dimensions of the plate heat exchanger based on
number of plates and gaps of water and refrigerant side, using this information to calculate cross sectional
area of flow and mass flux
• desuperheater_LP1-2.lib - Module containing information about the shell & tube desuperheater.
Total volume, shell volume and inner volume of tubes. Given number of tubes, inner diameter and
length of tubes, it calculates the cross sectional areas and mass fluxes in shell and tube side, based on
assumptions about staggered tube arrangements
• desuperheater_HP1-2.lib - Shell & tube desuperheater, calculated the same as for low pressure desu-
perheater
70
==PAGE:86==
• condenser_HP1-2.lib-Thecondensermodulecontainsinformationabouttheassumedlength,height
and thickness of each plate, and in the same fashion as the evaporator calculates the physical dimensions
of the condenser, based on number of plates, water and refrigerant side gaps.
• subcooler_HP1-2.lib - Shell & tube subcooler, calculated the same as with the desuperheaters
EES functions
An EES function was defined in the model, for controlling that certain signals, would stay in a given range. The
compressors were limited from 1500 to 3000 RPM for the high stage compressors as an example, and low stage
compressors from 1500 to 3600 RPM. Depending on the gains for the controllers and temperature error, the
controller could in some cases signal the capacity of the compressors to exceed their limits.
The limiter function works by three inputs, where a control signal is given, and a minimum and maximum
limit for the input signal. If the control signal is under the minimum, the output signal is set to the minimum
value and if the control signal exceeds the maximum value the output signal is set to the maximum value.
Correlation limits
The correlations for each component are only able to handle heat transfer in the thermodynamic conditions
they are valid for. For example, the condenser correlation is only valid for phase change, as the heat transfer is
heavily correlated to vapour quality. As such, the correlation isn’t valid during conditions, where the condenser
subcoolstherefrigerantaftercondensation. Thissituationwouldoccuratthecondenseroutletforsomeperiods
of time, during simulation. To handle the limits of the correlations, a procedure was introduced, where a check
function set the state at outlet to x = 0, before calling the correlation procedure. For the desuperheaters, the
refrigerant would in some cases enter condensation at outlet, and the same method was implemented to ensure
that the temperature at outlet was slightly higher than condensation temperature Tout = Tcond + 1e −3 as
temperature and pressure are constant during pahse change, and properties for the refrigerant can’t be found
from these alone.
Structure of computational model
The complete formulation of the EES model is formulated in sections for visual overview, for easier adjustment
and work flow. It requires loading a variety of custom made libraries containing an assortment of procedures
andmodulescontainingcorrelationsdeterminingtheheattransferconvectionscoefficientsaswellasthephysical
dimensions of the heat exchangers themselves. In addition to that. This is done in an effort to hide redundant
calculations and to preserve space and maintain a visual overview of the model itself. Each heat exchanger is
furthermore expressed in a module, giving an output of how much heat is transferred in the instant the module
is called, based on the massflows of the fluid, as well as the inlet and outlet states given to the module.
A description of each section of the model will be given, to give a structural overview of the model itself.
1st Section - Libraries
$INCLUDE directives - Loading libraries containing procedures and modules for correlations and physical
dimensions of heat exchangers. Defining number of intervals for heat exchanger discretisation.
2nd Section - Modules
Defining modules for calculating heat transfer of each heat exchanger. The modules take a number of various
inputs and gives a number of outputs. The inputs for the heat exchanger are physical dimensions, such as
number of plates and distance of gaps for the plate heat exchanger or number of, length of and inner diameter
of tubes. Other inputs necessary are types of fluids, pressure or salinity concentration, mass flows and inlet
and outlet states. In the modules are defined various other parameters, such as materials, plate thickness and
71
==PAGE:87==
corrugation angles. The modules prepare and discretise the inlet and outlet states for the heat exchanger for
calculating local convection coefficients along the heat transferring surfaces. Based on these coefficients, the local
U-value at each discretisation point is estimated and with the temperature difference as well as the local area, the
local heat transfer is estimated. Each local heat transfer is summed into a total and given as output from the
modules, along with the average U-value and average heat fluxes. At each discretisation point, the mass of the
fluid in the local volume is calculated as well, summed and given as output for use in the integral equation.
There a total of 10 modules, 5 for each heat pump, evaporator, low pressure desuperheater and high pressure
desuperheater, condenser and subcooler. The calculations are kept in a module, to afford an easy overview of
heat exchanger informations in separate arrays in the EES program.
The changing states along the heat transfer surfaces are considered to be linear in nature for the discretisation,
due to increased simplification of the model, whether it be change in temperature or change in vapour quality.
3rd Section - Functions
Auxiliary functions for help in control signals and values.
4th Section - System parameter deﬁnitions
Definition of various vital system parameters and cycle information. Evaporation, intermediary and conden-
sation temperatures and pressures are defined. κ is defined for the compressor calculations, optimal isentropic
efficiency, pump efficiency, fluid definitions, pressure and saline concentrations.
5th Section - Integral parameters
Integral table parameters. The end time for simulation and step size used for integration is defined, as well as
the variables for showing in the integral table.
6th Section - Control information
Parameters for use in the controller functions are defined here. Start-up conditions and setpoints are defined,
such as liquid levels in the vessels, valve positions and compressor RPM’s. Liquid separator, inter-cooler vessel
and condensation start-up temperatures and pressures. Compressor outlet temperatures, liquid separator cir-
culation number. The governing setpoints for the compressor capacity is defined here as well, as temperature
setpoints for the water temperature at specific points in the network of heat exchangers for heating the district
heating water.
7th section - PI control loops
This sections contains the 8 PI control loops for controlling the system on the refrigerant side. The variables
being controlled are, in order, the valve positions for controlling the condensation pressure, controlled by the
outlet state from the condenser.
The valve position for controlling the liquid level in the liquid separator, controlled by the volume of liquid
in the vessel.
Control loops for controlling the circulation of refrigerant for evaporation in the liquid vessel. Necessary for
the stability of the simulation and controlling the evaporation pressure in the liquid vessel.
Finally two controllers for the capacity of the compressor, governed by the temperature of district heating
water.
8th Section - LP1 compressor
Information for the low pressure compressor of heat pump 1, defining the theoretical swept volume, nominal
RPM for the compressor and volumetric compression ratio. Using inlet states for the compressor and pressure
ratiofortheevaporationandintermediarypressure,theisentropicandvolumetricefficienciesforthecompressor
iscalculatedby makingacallforthecompressor moduleloadedasalibraryfile. Withthevolumetricefficiency
72
==PAGE:88==
known, the compressor module also estimates the mass flow based on the state at compressor inlet giving it as
output from the compressor module.
Next the, a call for the oil_comp module is made, which estimates the compressor oil cooling, based on an
assumption of oil inlet temperature and volume flow, giving the outlet state from the compressor as well as the
heat transferred to the oil and electrical power consumed by the compressor.
Given information of the amount of heat cooled in the compressor by the oil, the heat transferred is used for
an integral equation in estimating the change in water temperature for the flow of water for cooling the oil and
district water heating.
9th Section - LP1 desuperheater
Definitions for the inlet and outlet flow for the low pressure desuperheater for heat pump 1. The refrigerant
inlet state is defined from the compressor outlet, and the water inlet is defined from the district heating network
model.
Physical dimensions for the shell & tube desuperheater is defined here. Number of tubes, length of tubes
and inner diameter of tubes. The outlet states of the fluids in the desuperheater is defined by integral equations,
which are initialized depending on initial state on the system. Given this information a call for the desuper-
heater_LP1 module is made, to calculate the heat transfer, which is used for the changing outlet states of the
fluids.
10th Section - LP1 Liquid Separator valve control
Declaration of an equation giving the mass flow from the intermediary vessel to the liquid separator, based
on valve characteristics and opening degree governed by a controller.
11th Section - LP1 Evaporator
This section declares information about the evaporator for Heat Pump 1. It contains assumptions about the
inlet and outlet states for the liquid separator connected to both the evaporative plate heat exchanger and the
liquid flow from the intercooler and outlet to the low stage compressor. The massflow through the evaporator
PHe is also defined, based on a variable circulation number and energy balance over the liquid separator tank.
With this information for the inlet to the evaporator, a call to the module with an initial guess on the outlet
state is made to the evaporator module, giving the outlet state.
12th Section - LP1 Liquid Separator
The liquid separator section contains the declarations giving the changing state of liquid and vapour inside
the vessel, based on initial values for the integral equations and the change in energy, volume and mass based
on the inlet and outlet flows in the liquid separator. This system of equations calculates the change of pressure,
liquid level, the evaporation of liquid due to superheated vapour and change of state for the vapour.
13th Section - HP1 Compressor
The HP1 compressor section calls the compressor module as well as the oil_comp module in the same
manner as for the LP1 compressor section, based on given information about the compressor dimensions and
revolutions, giving the outlet state from the compressor, work used and amount of heat from cooled by the oil.
14th Section - HP1 Condensation pressure valve control
The condensation pressure control, is declaration giving the valve characteristics, giving the change in con-
densation pressure, based on massflow and opening degree of the valve.
73
==PAGE:89==
15th Section - LP1 Inter cooler vessel
The intercooler vessel declaration gives the state of liquid and vapour in the intercooler in the same man-
ner as for the liquid separator. The inlet flows for the intercooler vessel are from the subcooler and low stage
desuperheater, the outlet flows are to the high stage compressor and liquid separator.
16th Section - HP1 Desuperheater
The declaration for the high stage desuperheater components states the inlet flows to the shell & tube desu-
perheater, receiving hot vapour from the high stage compressor, heating district heating water. Based on geo-
metrical definitions for the amount of tubes in a bundle, length and diameter, and given an initial outlet state,
this component calls the desuperheater HP1 module for calculating the heat transfer, giving the change in outlet
states.
17th Section - HP1 Condenser
The condenser HP1 component declaration uses the inlet conditions given from the outlet state from the
desuperheater, using them as input for the condenser HP1 module. With information about a chosen amount
of plates and gaps between plates on the refrigerant and water side, a call is made to the module, calculating heat
transfer, and the change in states along the length of the heat exchanger.
18th Section - HP1 Subcooler
The subcooler HP1 shell & tube component section uses the outlet from the condenser and makes a call to
the subcooler module, giving the heat transfer and change of outlet state.
19th through 29th Section - Heat pump 2
Repetition of Heat pump 1 sections, but with a higher condensation pressure and different dimensions for
the components.
30th Section - District water pump control
The section stating the equations for control of the district heating main water pump control. Based on the
setpoint temperatures for the wanted outlet temperature of water, the capacity of the main pump is adjusted
by a PI-control statement, giving the main flow of water for the network of heat exchangers heating the district
heating water.
31st Section - District water temperature setpoints and inlet temperatures
Statements giving the setpoints for individual outlet temperatures of water from each heat exchanger. The
given heat transfer capacity from the supplier is stated here and average heat capacity is calculated in this section.
Theinlettemperaturesforeachheatexchangerisdefinedinthissection, basedonthecalculationofoverallwater
temperatures in the water heat exchanger network.
32nd Section - District water ﬂow controllers
The water flow controllers for each heat exchanger is defined here, with a PI-controller for each heat ex-
changer, adjusting the flow based on a temperature water setpoint. When the water temperature increases too
much, the flow increases and vice versa. 10 controllers, 5 for each heat pump - 1 evaporator LP, 1 desuperheater
LP, 1 desuperheater HP, 1 condenser HP and 1 subcooler HP.
Given the massflow of water in each heat exchanger and the temperature at outlet, energy balances can be
established for the points in the district heat exchanger network, where the water is joined. 4 calls for 4 modules
contained in a library is made, one module for each group of heat exchangers, as seen in the overview of the
district water heat exchanger network.
74
==PAGE:90==
33rd Section - Delivered heat, work and efﬁciency
Given information and data from the model, calculations for estimating the amount of heat, work and pos-
sible cooling potential, is calculated here. Auxiliary pump work, compressor work, heat delivered is taken into
account, and the efficiency of the total system is found.
2.7 Start-up conditions
As the aim of the project is, for one thing, to be able to simulate a heat pump starting up and gain knowledge
of the ramp rate, the question of initial conditions have be considered.
Consideringtheheatpumpascompletelyoff,andhavinggainedthermodynamicequilibriumwithsurround-
ings, will involve the heat pump having temperature of around Tstart = 20 −25°℃. Refrigerant flow will have
settled and condenser in the liquid separator and intercooler, while the water in the heat exchangers will be at
equilibrium with surroundings.
As the heat pump starts up, the compressors will start at minimum speed, which is 1500 RPM, heating up
the water, while creating the pressure differentials by suction of vapour from the liquid separator and inter-
cooler, while increasing the condensation pressures by forcing refrigerant to the desuperheater, condenser and
subcooler with the valve at subcooler outlet constricting the flow, thereby increasing the pressure in conjunction
with the compressor, by increasing the amount of matter in the condensation stage. As such, the compressors
create the pressure differential in conjunction with the resistance (energy) from refrigerant matter in the rest
of the system. The high temperature created by the compression of vapour will transfer its heat to the colder
water, heating the water for delivery to the district heating network.
Thewatersidewillinitiallybeatthermodynamicequilibriumwithsurroundings. Butasthewatergetsheated
by the heat pump, the feed pump will start up, forcing water from the cold return of district heating water into
the system for heating.
Limits for initial conditions
The model isn’t able to handle starting up from conditions of true thermodynamic equilibrium. The pressures
in the liquid separator, intercooler and condenser are able to handle initial pressures. It is also seen during
verification of the vessels, that the pressures in the vessels change rapidly once the compressors starts sucking
out vapour. The heat exchanger models are not able to handle initial reverse heat fluxes, which change direction
duringsimulationrun. Thiswouldbeaproblemfortheevaporator, whereinitialpressureofevaporationwould
be warmer than the heat source temperature of water.
It was decided, for the start-up scenario, that the conditions would be ’near’ thermodynamic equilibrium,
meaning a short time scale after start-up. The water temperatures at all heat exchanger outlets would be equal to
water inlet temperature. As the compressors created low pressure in the liquid separator rapidly, the initial evap-
oration temperature in the liquid separator was chosen asTevap = Tsource−1K. Intercooler vessel pressure was
chosen as Tstage = 25°℃and condensation pressure was chosen as Tcond,H P1 = 60°℃and Tcond,H P1 = 70°℃.
The initial refrigerant outlet temperature from each heat exchanger were chosen as equal to inlet temperatures,
while the condenser outlet state was x = 0.9.
All massflows were initiated as a fraction of full load rated mass flows for the ehat exchangers 1
10 · Ûmrated,
while the compressor and feed pump capacities were set for minimum.
75
==PAGE:91==
3
Results - Heat Pump operation
Asthedifferentcomponentswereformulatedandintegratedintoacompletemodelformulation, itwasobvious
that each component needed to be verified in an isolated EES ennvironment to ensure verification of function-
ality, minimizing error sources from other parts of the system model. To ensure the components performed in
as wide a range of operations as possible, various mass flows and initial larges temperature differences were used
for testing.
3.1 Component heat transfer veriﬁcation
Verifying the heat transfer in the heat exchangers and the development of the temperatures behaved realisti-
cally was of high importance. Much of the work with the model was focused on modelling the heat exchanger
components in detail and ensuring that problematic conditions for the used correlations were handled to satis-
faction.
The heat exchangers where described in some geometric detail, where the heat transfer along the fluid flows
were discretised by dividing the internal volume of the fluids into smaller sub-volumes, and the state, mass
and energy density of each sub-volume treated separately. Two different formulations for heat transfer and the
state in each sub-volume was attempted. One where the state change in each sub-volume was described with a
differential equation and heat transfer to adjoining volumes and wall. Another where only the outlet state was
described with a differential equation and the states from inlet to outlet were assumed to progress in a linear
fashion.
As the condenser and evaporator are both of the PHE type, their model formulation were similar, while the
same is true for the SHT configuration of the desuperheater and the subcooler. Only one of each type will be
treated for verification. It should be noted, that the component verification are with constant mass flows, as
there is no variability introduced by compressor speed or water control. These isolated EES environments were
used for dimensioning the components for their steady state loads as well.
Desuperheater
The desuperheater HP2 is heat exchanger of the shell & tube configuration. For the verification of this heat
exchanger the following initial conditions apply:
• Condensation temperature:
Tcond = 80°℃
• Refrigerant mass flow, constant:
Ûmref = 1.92 kg/s
• Water mass flow, constant:
Ûmw = 3.99 kg/s
• Initial temperature on refrigerant side:
Tref = 100°℃
• Initial temperature on water side:
Tw = 78°℃

Model with numerical errors
Initially, the method with differential equations for each sub-volume was preferred, as it gave a more exact re-
sult for the heat transfer and temperatures along the fluid flows in the heat exchangers. But as the full model
formulation unfortunately presented numerical errors, the linearised method was chosen instead for the model
formulation. Initially, the numerical errors were handled, by storing the last values of a simulation run, using
them to initialise a new simulation.

3.2 Condenser control veriﬁcation
For the condenser pressure, a PI-controller was used, to simulate a valve increasing or decreasing the pressure,
to match the outlet state from the condensers to that of of saturated liquid. Figure 3.21 shows the saturation
controller increasing the condensation pressure. From an initial condensation temperature ofTcond = 68°℃the
pressure starts to increase, to make sure that the outlet state is saturated. A minimum value for the controller
to initiate was stated as xmin = 0.85 to ensure a stable initial condenser pressure, from which the controller
would start shortly after starting simulation. The signalled error is shown in the greyed out area, with the valve
responding
Figure 3.21: Condenser saturation pressure control
As a safety precaution another controller for the condenser pressure was implemented as well, based on the
pinch point of the condenser, the temperature difference between the condensation temperature and the water
outlet. In reality, this temperature isn’t used for controlled the condensation pressure. Preferably the controller
87
==PAGE:103==
should never activate, and was only implemented in case of the water outlet temperature becoming too high,
for the sake of safekeeping model stability. Figure 3.22 shows the pinch point control, where the greyed area
is PP = Tcond −1. The water temperature is shown as never reaching this area, and thus the controller not
activating.
Figure 3.22: pressure control to ensure pinch point in condenser

3.6 System model veriﬁcation
As each component was verified and implemented in the full model formulation, the full model required verifi-
cationaswell, toensureintendedfunction, andkeepingwithinfeasiblelimitsandconditionsofthermodynamic
laws. For the full model simulation, each individual controller for each component is preoccupied with match-
ing their governing process variables with their set point, by varying a manipulable variable, such as water flow,
valve setting or capacity. The main components governing the overall capacity and heat capacity of the two
parallel two stage ammonia heat pumps are the pairs of compressors and the main feed pump for the district
heating water.
The main set points that govern the compressor and feed pump capacities are given below and are valid in all
instances of simulations:
• Heat pump 1 temperature setpoint:
TV P1,sp = 67°℃
• Heat pump 2 temperature setpoint:
TV P2,sp = 80°℃
• Water outlet temperature setpoint:
TDH,sp = 80°℃
• Feed pump temperature setpoint:
TDH,of f set = 79°℃
Steady state
For the steady state scenario, the system model was initialised with all temperatures at their given set points, and
all flows at their rated flows for dimensioned heat transfer. The condenser water outlet temperatures were ini-
tialisedwiththecalculateddimensiontemperaturessuppliedinthebudgetquotationfromGEAinAppendixC
and all valves were set to their dimensioned opening degree. After initiation of simulation, all regulators were
free to match their process variables to their set points.
• Capacity for compressors and main flow pump:
100%

• All water outlet temperatures:

• All heat exchanger water flows:

• Intercooler temperature:

• Condensation temperature HP2:

with the consumed power from compressors and pumps is shown, giving the COP as well. The supplied heat
capacity from the heat pump is seen to offer slightly over 5 MW, as is the rated heat capacity of the SVAF heat
pump, also with a COP of around 3.3 which corresponds very well with the stated heat pump from the SVAF
project.

Start-up veriﬁcation
Start up of the heat pump, is one of the scenarios, from which information on the operation of the heat pump is
wanted. As mentioned, the heat pump model did not have the capability to initiate simulation, from complete
thermodynamic equilibrium with surroundings, assumed to be Tamb = 20 −25°℃. Instead it was chosen to
start from a state of ’near’ equilibrium, where the heat pump is assumed to be, just after initial heating of the
components. The lowest temperature in the district water heating exchangers is thus chosen as the cold water
return from the district heating network TDH,in = 50°℃. The condensation temperatures are chosen to be
∆T = 10 below their steady state operation. With the heat pump thus initiated, start up operation is simulated
for verification.
This assumption of ’near’ start up, is assumed to be within reasonable limits, as the temperatures for the heat
99
==PAGE:115==
exchangers in the earlier verification for the desuperheater HP2 and the condenser HP2 were seen to develop
rapidly towards steady temperatures
• Capacity for compressors:
50%
• Capacity for feed pump:
42%
• All water outlet temperatures:
50°℃
• All heat exchanger water flows:
0.1 · Ûmf ull,load
• Evaporation temperature:
Tevap = −2°℃
• Intercooler temperature:
Tstage = 25°℃
• Condensation temperature HP1:
Tcond,H P1 = 60°℃
• Condensation temperature HP2:
Tcond,H P2 = 70°℃
• Liquid Separator liquid level:
fliqsep = 0.3

fvessel = 0.7
The performance and heat capacity for the heat pumps can be seen in Figure 3.42.

==PAGE:116==
Reduce load veriﬁcation
The reverse case, where the heat pump needs to change load or shut down, is also investigated for verification.
In cases where the heat pump needs to supply lower heat to the district heating network or change load from
one load to another, it is valuable to know how rapidly the heat pump is able to perform the load change. For
the reduction of load scenario, the heat pump would be assumed to be operating at full load, from which it
would have to reduce the heat capacity until the lowest load possible for the heat pump. The initial state of the
heat pump is thus the same as for the steady state scenario.
To make the heat pump perform this action in simulation, the setpoint temperatures for the compressors
and feed pump were reversed, as to make the compressors attempt the reduce the water outlet temperature to
TV P,of f set = TV P,sp −1, while the feed pump would attempt to match the outlet temperature to TDH,out =
80°℃. The initial state of the heat pump is as follows.
• Capacity for compressors and main flow pump:
100%
• All water outlet temperatures:
Set point
• All heat exchanger water flows:
Ûmf ull,load
• Evaporation temperature:
Tevap = −2°℃
• Intercooler temperature:
Tstage = 35°℃
• Condensation temperature HP1:
Tcond,H P1 = 67°℃
• Condensation temperature HP2:
Tcond,H P2 = 80°℃
• Liquid Separator liquid level:
fliqsep = 0.3
• Intercooler liquid level:
fvessel = 0.7
The results of the simulation scenario is seen in Figure 3.43, where heat pump is seen to reduce the supplied
heat to the district heating network, as the compressors and feed pump reduce their capacity.

3.7 Validation
For every numerical model, a validation of the simulation performance is desired, to ensure the thermodynamic
phenomena of the model are concurrent with real life measurements.
Unfortunately, as no specific information on start up times of large scale industrial heat pumps are available,
besides what the industry may keep on hand, real validation of the model could not be performed. A typical
interval that was mentioned for the heat pump was around 5-10 minutes for the heat pump to start up and
perform at full load capacity. This interval is concurrent with the simulated start up time of the heat pump,
shown in Figure 3.42, where 5 MW of heat capacity is reached around the 10 min mark.
102

4.2 Load change of Heat pump
Changing the load of the heat pump for part load, can be required ins certain situation. for those cases it is im-
portant to know, how fast the heat pump can reduce the heat capacity it delivers to the district heating network.
Fortheloadreduction,thesetpointwhichthecompressorsandwaterfeedpumpsshouldmatch,were’reversed’,
meaning that the compressors would now attempt to match the temperature offset ofTof f set = Tsp −1, while
the main water feed pump would attempt to match the outlet temperature of water to Tsp = 80°℃, giving the
results as shown.
109
==PAGE:125==
The reduction of load can be seen in Figure 4.14, where the heat capacity is seen to decrease, while the overall
consumed power goes down as well, due to the lower work required for compression and pumping. this results
in the COP increasing slightly, before stabilizing in steady state low load.

4.3 Increasing ramp rate
To test the possibility of starting the heat pumps faster, the gains of the compressors were increased from their
initial verification gains. Initially each of the compressors had a gain of Kp = 30 · e(t) where the error is
e(t) = ∆Ts p
0.1 . The feed pump had an initial gain of Kp = 20 · e(t) where the error is e(t) = ∆Ts p
0.1 . The gains
shown in the legends of the following results, are thus added to the base gains. Increasing the gain in increments
of 5, gave the following results for the start up and supplied heat of the heat pump, as shown in Figure 4.24.
The heat supplied can be seen to initially have the same development, but as the 3 min mark is passed, the ramp
rate differs significantly. The initial development of the supplied heat, is due to the conditions implemented for
114
==PAGE:130==
the compressors and feed pump, ensuring they stay at minimum capacity until a minimum temperature for the
water has been reached. This was done, as the model would otherwise diverge, as the error in temperature was
simply too large, making the capacities jump in value too rapidly. A method was to lower the gains, but then
the heat pump would ramp much too slowly. A different method or condition should perhaps be implemented
for the controller.
